<ul><li><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="215" data-file-width="206" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/15px-Allah-green.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/23px-Allah-green.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/31px-Allah-green.svg.png 2x" width="15"/>Â <a href="/wiki/Portal:Islam" title="Portal:Islam">Islam portal</a></li>
<li><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="1911" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/69/P_vip.svg/16px-P_vip.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/69/P_vip.svg/24px-P_vip.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/69/P_vip.svg/31px-P_vip.svg.png 2x" width="16"/>Â <a href="/wiki/Portal:Biography" title="Portal:Biography">Biography portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar" style="background:white; padding:0 5px"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Muhammad" title="Template:Muhammad"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Muhammad" title="Template talk:Muhammad"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Muhammad&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Muhammad ibn Abdullah</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[n 1]</a></sup> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ÙÙØ­ÙÙÙÙØ¯ Ù±Ø¨Ù Ø¹ÙØ¨ÙØ¯ Ù±ÙÙÙÙÙ°Ù</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Arabic" title="Romanization of Arabic">romanized</a>:Â </small><span title="Arabic-language romanization"><i lang="ar-Latn">Muá¸¥ammad ibn Ê¿Abd AllÄh</i></span>, <span style="font-size:85%;">Classical Arabic pronunciation:</span> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="ar-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[muËÄ§ammad]</a></span>; <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>Â 570 â 8 June 632 CE)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abraha_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-abraha-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldman_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldman-3">[2]</a></sup> was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arab</a> religious, social, and political leader and the founder of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_religion" title="World religion">world religion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEIW_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-OEIW-4">[3]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">Islamic doctrine</a>, he was a <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a> <a href="/wiki/Divine_inspiration" title="Divine inspiration">divinely inspired</a> to preach and confirm the <a href="/wiki/Tawhid" title="Tawhid">monotheistic</a> teachings of <a href="/wiki/Adam_in_Islam" title="Adam in Islam">Adam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_in_Islam" title="Abraham in Islam">Abraham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moses_in_Islam" title="Moses in Islam">Moses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">Jesus</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">prophets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEIW_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-OEIW-4">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> He is believed to be the <a href="/wiki/Seal_of_the_Prophets" title="Seal of the Prophets">Seal of the Prophets</a> within Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[n 2]</a></sup> Muhammad united <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabia</a> into a single Muslim <a href="/wiki/Polity" title="Polity">polity</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> as well as his teachings and practices forming the basis of Islamic religious belief.
</p><p>Muhammad was born approximately 570<span class="nowrap">Â </span>CE in <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abraha_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-abraha-1">[1]</a></sup> He was the son of <a href="/wiki/Abdullah_ibn_Abd_al-Muttalib" title="Abdullah ibn Abd al-Muttalib">Abdullah ibn Abd al-Muttalib</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amina_bint_Wahb" title="Amina bint Wahb">Amina bint Wahb</a>. His father Abdullah was the son of <a href="/wiki/Quraysh" title="Quraysh">Quraysh</a> tribal leader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abd_al-Muttalib_ibn_Hashim" title="Abd al-Muttalib ibn Hashim">Abd al-Muttalib ibn Hashim</a>, and he died a few months before Muhammad's birth. His mother Amina died when he was six, leaving Muhammad an orphan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[7]</a></sup> He was raised under the care of his grandfather, Abd al-Muttalib, and paternal uncle, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Talib_ibn_Abd_al-Muttalib" title="Abu Talib ibn Abd al-Muttalib">Abu Talib</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt7_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watt7-10">[8]</a></sup> In later years, he would periodically seclude himself in a mountain cave named <a href="/wiki/Jabal_al-Nour" title="Jabal al-Nour">Hira</a> for several nights of prayer. When he was 40, Muhammad reported being visited by <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">Gabriel</a> in the cave<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abraha_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-abraha-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncWorldHistory_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncWorldHistory-11">[9]</a></sup> and receiving <a href="/wiki/Muhammad%27s_first_revelation" title="Muhammad's first revelation">his first revelation</a> from God. In 613,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[10]</a></sup> Muhammad started <a href="/wiki/Dawah" title="Dawah">preaching</a> these revelations publicly,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al-A'zami2_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Al-A'zami2-13">[11]</a></sup> proclaiming that "<a href="/wiki/Tawhid" title="Tawhid">God is One</a>", that complete "submission" (<i><a href="/wiki/Islam#Etymology" title="Islam">islÄm</a></i>) to God<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup> is the right way of life (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D%C4%ABn" title="DÄ«n">dÄ«n</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup> and that he was a prophet and messenger of God, similar to the other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam#Table_of_prophets/messengers_in_the_Quran" title="Prophets in Islam">prophets in Islam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peters_2003_9_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peters_2003_9-16">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EspositoI_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-EspositoI-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Muhammad's <a href="/wiki/Companions_of_the_Prophet" title="Companions of the Prophet">followers</a> were initially few in number, and experienced <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Muslims_by_Meccans" title="Persecution of Muslims by Meccans">hostility from Meccan polytheists</a> for 13 years. To escape ongoing persecution, he <a href="/wiki/Migration_to_Abyssinia" title="Migration to Abyssinia">sent some of his followers</a> to <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aksum" title="Kingdom of Aksum">Abyssinia</a> in 615, before he and his followers migrated from Mecca to <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> (then known as Yathrib) later in 622. This event, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegira" title="Hegira">Hijra</a></i>, marks the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Islamic calendar</a>, also known as the Hijri Calendar. In Medina, Muhammad united the tribes under the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Medina" title="Constitution of Medina">Constitution of Medina</a>. In December 629, after eight years of intermittent fighting with Meccan tribes, Muhammad gathered an army of 10,000 Muslim converts and <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_Mecca" title="Conquest of Mecca">marched on the city of Mecca</a>. The conquest went largely uncontested and Muhammad seized the city with little bloodshed. In 632, a few months after returning from the <a href="/wiki/Farewell_Pilgrimage" title="Farewell Pilgrimage">Farewell Pilgrimage</a>, he fell ill and died. By the time of his death, most of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Islam" title="Conversion to Islam">converted to Islam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lapidus_2002_pp_0_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lapidus_2002_pp_0-20">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The revelations (each known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayah" title="Ayah">Ayah</a></i>Â â  literally, "Sign [of God]") that Muhammad reported receiving until his death form the verses of the Quran, regarded by Muslims as the verbatim "Word of God" on which the religion is based. Besides the Quran, Muhammad's teachings and practices (<i><a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a></i>), found in the <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Prophetic_biography" title="Prophetic biography">sira</a></i> (biography) literature, are also upheld and used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sources_of_sharia" title="Sources of sharia">sources</a> of Islamic law (see <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a>).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Quranic_names_and_appellations">Quranic names and appellations</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Names_and_titles_of_Muhammad" title="Names and titles of Muhammad">Names and titles of Muhammad</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Salat.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="303" data-file-width="538" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Muhammad_Salat.svg/220px-Muhammad_Salat.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Muhammad_Salat.svg/330px-Muhammad_Salat.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Muhammad_Salat.svg/440px-Muhammad_Salat.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Salat.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Muhammad" written in <a href="/wiki/Thuluth" title="Thuluth">Thuluth</a>, a script variety of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">Islamic calligraphy</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_(name)" title="Muhammad (name)">name <i>Muhammad</i></a> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/Ê/: 'u' in 'push'">Ê</span><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/Ã¦/: 'a' in 'bad'">Ã¦</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/É/: 'a' in 'about'">É</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ÉË/: 'a' in 'father'">ÉË</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/É/: 'a' in 'about'">É</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> means "praiseworthy" and appears four times in the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup> The Quran also addresses Muhammad in the second person by various appellations; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" title="Prophets in Islam">prophet</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostle_(Islam)" title="Apostle (Islam)">messenger</a>, servant of God ('<i>abd</i>), announcer (<i>bashir</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup> witness (<i><a href="/wiki/Shahid_(name)" title="Shahid (name)">shahid</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup> bearer of good tidings (<i>mubashshir</i>), warner (<i>nathir</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> reminder (<i>mudhakkir</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[24]</a></sup> one who calls [unto God] (<i><a href="/wiki/Dawah" title="Dawah">dÄ'Ä«</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> light personified (<i>noor</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> and the light-giving lamp (<i>siraj munir</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources_of_biographical_information">Sources of biographical information</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_early_Islam" title="Historiography of early Islam">Historiography of early Islam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Historicity_of_Muhammad" title="Historicity of Muhammad">Historicity of Muhammad</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quran">Quran</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Folio_from_a_Koran_(8th-9th_century).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2448" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Folio_from_a_Koran_%288th-9th_century%29.jpg/220px-Folio_from_a_Koran_%288th-9th_century%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Folio_from_a_Koran_%288th-9th_century%29.jpg/330px-Folio_from_a_Koran_%288th-9th_century%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Folio_from_a_Koran_%288th-9th_century%29.jpg/440px-Folio_from_a_Koran_%288th-9th_century%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Folio_from_a_Koran_(8th-9th_century).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A folio from an early <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, written in <a href="/wiki/Kufic" title="Kufic">Kufic</a> script (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasid" title="Abbasid">Abbasid</a> period, 8thâ9th centuries)</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> is the central <a href="/wiki/Religious_text" title="Religious text">religious text</a> of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>. Muslims believe it represents the words of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a> revealed by the archangel <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">Gabriel</a> to Muhammad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-30">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LivRlgP338_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-LivRlgP338-31">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> The Quran, however, provides minimal assistance for Muhammad's chronological biography; most Quranic verses do not provide significant historical context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennett1998a_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bennett1998a-33">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peters1994_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peters1994-34">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_biographies">Early biographies</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prophetic_biography" title="Prophetic biography">Prophetic biography</a></div>
<p>Important sources regarding Muhammad's life may be found in the historic works by writers of the 2nd and 3rd centuries <a href="/wiki/Hijri_year" title="Hijri year">of the Muslim era</a> (AHÂ â 8th and 9th century CE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt-Mecca-xi_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watt-Mecca-xi-35">[33]</a></sup> These include traditional Muslim biographies of Muhammad, which provide additional information about Muhammad's life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reeves_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reeves-36">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest written <i>sira</i> (biographies of Muhammad and quotes attributed to him) is <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Ishaq" title="Ibn Ishaq">Ibn Ishaq</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Prophetic_biography" title="Prophetic biography">Life of God's Messenger</a></i> written c. 767 CE (150 AH). Although the original work was lost, this sira survives as extensive excerpts in works by <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hisham" title="Ibn Hisham">Ibn Hisham</a> and to a lesser extent by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Jarir_al-Tabari" title="Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari">Al-Tabari</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nigosian6_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nigosian6-37">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> However, Ibn Hisham wrote in the preface to his biography of Muhammad that he omitted matters from Ibn Ishaq's biography that "would distress certain people".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> Another early history source is the history of Muhammad's campaigns by <a href="/wiki/Al-Waqidi" title="Al-Waqidi">al-Waqidi</a> (death 207 AH), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Book_of_the_Major_Classes" title="The Book of the Major Classes">the work</a> of Waqidi's secretary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Sa%27d_al-Baghdadi" title="Ibn Sa'd al-Baghdadi">Ibn Sa'd al-Baghdadi</a> (death 230 AH).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt-Mecca-xi_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watt-Mecca-xi-35">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many scholars accept these early biographies as authentic, though their accuracy is unascertainable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nigosian6_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nigosian6-37">[35]</a></sup> Recent studies have led scholars to distinguish between traditions touching legal matters and purely historical events. In the legal group, traditions could have been subject to invention while historic events, aside from exceptional cases, may have been only subject to "tendential shaping".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hadith">Hadith</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a></div>
<p>Other important sources include the <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a> collections, accounts of verbal and physical teachings and traditions attributed to Muhammad. Hadiths were compiled several generations after his death by Muslims including <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Bukhari" title="Muhammad al-Bukhari">Muhammad al-Bukhari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muslim_ibn_al-Hajjaj" title="Muslim ibn al-Hajjaj">Muslim ibn al-Hajjaj</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Isa_at-Tirmidhi" title="Muhammad ibn Isa at-Tirmidhi">Muhammad ibn Isa at-Tirmidhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Nasa%27i" title="Al-Nasa'i">Abd ar-Rahman al-Nasai</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Dawood" title="Abu Dawood">Abu Dawood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Majah" title="Ibn Majah">Ibn Majah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malik_ibn_Anas" title="Malik ibn Anas">Malik ibn Anas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Daraqutni" title="Al-Daraqutni">al-Daraqutni</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_1993,_pp._33â34_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis_1993,_pp._33â34-41">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some Western academics cautiously view the hadith collections as accurate historical sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_1993,_pp._33â34_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis_1993,_pp._33â34-41">[39]</a></sup> Scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Wilferd_Madelung" title="Wilferd Madelung">Madelung</a> do not reject the narrations which have been compiled in later periods, but judge them in the context of history and on the basis of their compatibility with the events and figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> Muslim scholars on the other hand typically place a greater emphasis on the hadith literature instead of the biographical literature, since hadiths maintain a traditional chain of transmission (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isnad" title="Isnad">isnad</a>); the lack of such a chain for the biographical literature makes it unverifiable in their eyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Islamic_Arabia">Pre-Islamic Arabia</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Pre-Islamic_Arabia" title="Pre-Islamic Arabia">Pre-Islamic Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jahiliyyah" title="Jahiliyyah">Jahiliyyah</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_pre-Islamic_Arabia" title="Religion in pre-Islamic Arabia">Religion in pre-Islamic Arabia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tribes_english.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="828" data-file-width="1014" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Tribes_english.png/220px-Tribes_english.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Tribes_english.png/330px-Tribes_english.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Tribes_english.png/440px-Tribes_english.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tribes_english.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Main tribes and settlements of Arabia in Muhammad's lifetime</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> was, and still is, largely arid with volcanic soil, making agriculture difficult except near oases or springs. Towns and cities dotted the landscape, two of the most prominent being <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>. Medina was a large flourishing agricultural settlement, while Mecca was an important financial center for many surrounding tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muhammad-Mecca-12_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Muhammad-Mecca-12-45">[43]</a></sup> Communal life was essential for survival in the <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert</a> conditions, supporting indigenous tribes against the harsh environment and lifestyle. Tribal affiliation, whether based on kinship or alliances, was an important source of social cohesion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup> Indigenous Arabs were either <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sedentism" title="Sedentism">sedentary</a>. Nomadic groups constantly traveled seeking water and pasture for their flocks, while the sedentary settled and focused on trade and agriculture. Nomadic survival also depended on raiding caravans or oases; nomads did not view this as a crime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rue_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rue-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esposito4_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Esposito4-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>In pre-Islamic Arabia, gods or goddesses were viewed as protectors of individual tribes, their spirits associated with sacred trees, <a href="/wiki/Baetylus" title="Baetylus">stones</a>, springs and wells. As well as being the site of an annual pilgrimage, the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a> shrine in Mecca housed 360 idols of tribal patron deities. Three goddesses were worshipped, in some places as daughters of Allah: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All%C4%81t" title="AllÄt">AllÄt</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Man%C4%81t" title="ManÄt">ManÄt</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-%27Uzz%C3%A1" title="Al-'UzzÃ¡">al-'UzzÃ¡</a>. Monotheistic communities existed in Arabia, including Christians and <a href="/wiki/Jewish_tribes_of_Arabia" title="Jewish tribes of Arabia">Jews</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hanif" title="Hanif">Hanifs</a> â native pre-Islamic Arabs who "professed a rigid monotheism"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup> â are also sometimes listed alongside Jews and Christians in pre-Islamic Arabia, although scholars dispute their <a href="/wiki/Historicity_(philosophy)" title="Historicity (philosophy)">historicity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> According to Muslim tradition, Muhammad himself was a Hanif and one of the descendants of <a href="/wiki/Ishmael" title="Ishmael">Ishmael</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> After a century of exhaustive archaeological investigation, no evidence has been found of a historical Abraham or Ishmael.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>The second half of the sixth century was a period of political disorder in Arabia and communication routes were no longer secure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup> Religious divisions were an important cause of the crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robin302_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robin302-56">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> became the dominant religion in <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a> while Christianity took root in the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robin302_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robin302-56">[54]</a></sup> In line with broader trends of the ancient world, the region witnessed a decline in the practice of polytheistic cults and a growing interest in a more spiritual form of religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robin302_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-Robin302-56">[54]</a></sup> While many were reluctant to convert to a foreign faith, those faiths provided intellectual and spiritual reference points.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robin302_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-Robin302-56">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the early years of Muhammad's life, the <a href="/wiki/Quraysh" title="Quraysh">Quraysh</a> tribe to which he belonged became a dominant force in western Arabia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robin286_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robin286-57">[55]</a></sup> They formed the cult association of <i>hums</i>, which tied members of many tribes in western Arabia to the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a> and reinforced the prestige of the Meccan sanctuary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robin301_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robin301-58">[56]</a></sup> To counter the effects of anarchy, Quraysh upheld the institution of sacred months during which all violence was forbidden, and it was possible to participate in pilgrimages and fairs without danger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robin301_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robin301-58">[56]</a></sup> Thus, although the association of <i>hums</i> was primarily religious, it also had important economic consequences for the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robin301_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-Robin301-58">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_and_early_life">Childhood and early life</span></h3>

<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Site_of_the_birthplace_of_the_Prophet_Muhammad,_Mecca,_Saudi_Arabia.jpg"><img alt="" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Makkah_Al_Mukarramah_Library" title="Makkah Al Mukarramah Library">Makkah Al Mukarramah Library</a> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1073938472" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Muhammad&amp;params=21_25_30_N_39_49_48_E_type:landmark_scale:5000_region:SA&amp;title=Bayt+al-Mawlid+%2F+Makkah+Al+Mukarramah+Library"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">21Â°25â²30â³N</span> <span class="longitude">39Â°49â²48â³E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">ï»¿ / ï»¿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">21.42500Â°N 39.83000Â°E</span><span style="display:none">ï»¿ / <span class="geo">21.42500; 39.83000</span></span><span style="display:none">ï»¿ (<span class="fn org">Bayt al-Mawlid / Makkah Al Mukarramah Library</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>) is believed to stand on the spot where Muhammad was <a href="/wiki/Mawlid" title="Mawlid">born</a>, so it is also known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bayt_al-Mawlid" title="Bayt al-Mawlid">Bayt al-Mawlid</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Abu al-Qasim Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn Abd al-Muttalib ibn Hashim<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-59">[57]</a></sup> was born in Mecca<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> about the year 570<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abraha_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-abraha-1">[1]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mawlid" title="Mawlid">his birthday</a> is believed to be in the month of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rabi%27_al-awwal" title="Rabi' al-awwal">Rabi' al-awwal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup> He belonged to the <a href="/wiki/Banu_Hashim" title="Banu Hashim">Banu Hashim</a> clan, part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quraysh_tribe" title="Quraysh tribe">Quraysh tribe</a>, which was one of <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>'s prominent families, although it appears less prosperous during Muhammad's early lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup> Tradition places the year of Muhammad's birth as corresponding with the <a href="/wiki/Year_of_the_Elephant" title="Year of the Elephant">Year of the Elephant</a>, which is named after the failed destruction of Mecca that year by the <a href="/wiki/Abraha" title="Abraha">Abraha</a>, Yemen's king, who supplemented his army with elephants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup>
Alternatively some 20th century scholars have suggested different years, such as 568 or 569.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt7_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watt7-10">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="depiction">
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mohammed_kaaba_1315.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1470" data-file-width="2014" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Mohammed_kaaba_1315.jpg/220px-Mohammed_kaaba_1315.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Mohammed_kaaba_1315.jpg/330px-Mohammed_kaaba_1315.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Mohammed_kaaba_1315.jpg/440px-Mohammed_kaaba_1315.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mohammed_kaaba_1315.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Miniature from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rashid-al-Din_Hamadani" title="Rashid-al-Din Hamadani">Rashid-al-Din Hamadani</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jami_al-Tawarikh" title="Jami al-Tawarikh">Jami al-Tawarikh</a></i>, <span title="circa">c.</span><span style="white-space:nowrap;">â1315</span>, illustrating the story of Muhammad's role in re-setting the <a href="/wiki/Black_Stone" title="Black Stone">Black Stone</a> in 605. (<a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a> period)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup></div></div></div>
</div>
<p>Muhammad's father, <a href="/wiki/Abdullah_ibn_Abd_al-Muttalib" title="Abdullah ibn Abd al-Muttalib">Abdullah</a>, died almost six months before he was born.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meri2004_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meri2004-67">[65]</a></sup> According to Islamic tradition, soon after birth he was sent to live with a <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> family in the desert, as desert life was considered healthier for infants; some western scholars reject this tradition's historicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WattHalimah_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-WattHalimah-68">[66]</a></sup> Muhammad stayed with his foster-mother, <a href="/wiki/Halimah_bint_Abi_Dhuayb" title="Halimah bint Abi Dhuayb">Halimah bint Abi Dhuayb</a>, and her husband until he was two years old. At the age of six, Muhammad lost his biological mother <a href="/wiki/Aminah" title="Aminah">Amina</a> to illness and became an orphan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WattHalimah_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-WattHalimah-68">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup> For the next two years, until he was eight years old, Muhammad was under the guardianship of his paternal grandfather <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Muttalib" title="Abd al-Muttalib">Abd al-Muttalib</a>, of the Banu Hashim clan until his death. He then came under the care of his uncle <a href="/wiki/Abu_Talib_ibn_Abd_al-Muttalib" title="Abu Talib ibn Abd al-Muttalib">Abu Talib</a>, the new leader of the Banu Hashim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt7_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Watt7-10">[8]</a></sup> According to Islamic historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Montgomery_Watt" title="William Montgomery Watt">William Montgomery Watt</a> there was a general disregard by guardians in taking care of weaker members of the tribes in Mecca during the 6th century, "Muhammad's guardians saw that he did not starve to death, but it was hard for them to do more for him, especially as the fortunes of the clan of Hashim seem to have been declining at that time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt8_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watt8-70">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his teens, Muhammad accompanied his uncle on Syrian trading journeys to gain experience in commercial trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt8_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watt8-70">[68]</a></sup> Islamic tradition states that when Muhammad was either nine or twelve while accompanying the Meccans' caravan to Syria, he met a Christian monk or hermit named <a href="/wiki/Bahira" title="Bahira">Bahira</a> who is said to have foreseen Muhammad's career as a prophet of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Little is known of Muhammad during his later youth as available information is fragmented, making it difficult to separate history from legend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt8_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-Watt8-70">[68]</a></sup> It is known that he became a merchant and "was involved in trade between the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BerkWorldHistory_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-BerkWorldHistory-72">[70]</a></sup> Due to his upright character he acquired the nickname "<a href="/wiki/Amin_(name)" title="Amin (name)">al-Amin</a>" (Arabic: Ø§ÙØ§ÙÙÙ), meaning "faithful, trustworthy" and "al-Sadiq" meaning "truthful"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> and was sought out as an impartial arbitrator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncWorldHistory_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-EncWorldHistory-11">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup> His reputation attracted a proposal in 595 from <a href="/wiki/Khadija_bint_Khuwaylid" title="Khadija bint Khuwaylid">Khadijah</a>, a successful businesswoman. Muhammad consented to the marriage, which by all accounts was a happy one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BerkWorldHistory_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-BerkWorldHistory-72">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several years later, according to a narration collected by historian <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Ishaq" title="Ibn Ishaq">Ibn Ishaq</a>, Muhammad was involved with a well-known story about setting the <a href="/wiki/Black_Stone" title="Black Stone">Black Stone</a> in place in the wall of the Kaaba in 605 CE. The Black Stone, a sacred object, was removed during renovations to the Kaaba. The Meccan leaders could not agree which clan should return the Black Stone to its place. They decided to ask the next man who came through the gate to make that decision; that man was the 35-year-old Muhammad. This event happened five years before the first revelation by Gabriel to him. He asked for a cloth and laid the Black Stone in its center. The clan leaders held the corners of the cloth and together carried the Black Stone to the right spot, then Muhammad laid the stone, satisfying the honor of all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dairesi_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dairesi-75">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beginnings_of_the_Quran">Beginnings of the Quran</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Muhammad%27s_first_revelation" title="Muhammad's first revelation">Muhammad's first revelation</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Quran" title="History of the Quran">History of the Quran</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wahy" title="Wahy">Wahy</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" â ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" â ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30%; ; background-color: #ffeeaa;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p><i>Recite in the name of your Lord who createdâCreated man from a clinging substance. Recite, and your Lord is the most GenerousâWho taught by the penâTaught man that which he knew not.</i>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">â <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quran_96:1%E2%80%935" title="Quran 96:1â5">Quran 96:1â5</a></cite>
</p>
</div>
<div class="depiction">
<p>Muhammad began to pray alone in a cave named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cave_of_Hira" title="Cave of Hira">Hira</a> on <a href="/wiki/Jabal_al-Nour" title="Jabal al-Nour">Mount Jabal al-Nour</a>, near Mecca for several weeks every year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup> Islamic tradition holds that during one of his visits to that cave, in the year 610 the angel <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">Gabriel</a> appeared to him and commanded Muhammad to recite verses that would be included in the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup> Consensus exists that the first Quranic words revealed were the beginning of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quran_96:1" title="Quran 96:1">Quran 96:1</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EI2-Wahy_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-EI2-Wahy-80">[78]</a></sup>
Muhammad was deeply distressed upon receiving his first revelations. After returning home, Muhammad was consoled and reassured by Khadijah and her Christian cousin, <a href="/wiki/Waraqah_ibn_Nawfal" title="Waraqah ibn Nawfal">Waraqah ibn Nawfal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-81">[79]</a></sup> He also feared that others would dismiss his claims as being possessed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esposito4_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Esposito4-48">[46]</a></sup> Shi'a tradition states Muhammad was not surprised or frightened at Gabriel's appearance; rather he welcomed the angel, as if he was expected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> The initial revelation was followed by a three-year pause (a period known as <i>fatra</i>) during which Muhammad felt depressed and further gave himself to prayers and <a href="/wiki/Spiritual_practice" title="Spiritual practice">spiritual practices</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EI2-Wahy_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-EI2-Wahy-80">[78]</a></sup> When the revelations resumed he was reassured and commanded to begin preaching: "Thy Guardian-Lord hath not forsaken thee, nor is He displeased."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cave_Hira.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1976" data-file-width="1721" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Cave_Hira.jpg/170px-Cave_Hira.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Cave_Hira.jpg/255px-Cave_Hira.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Cave_Hira.jpg/340px-Cave_Hira.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cave_Hira.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The cave <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cave_of_Hira" title="Cave of Hira">Hira</a> in the mountain <a href="/wiki/Jabal_al-Nour" title="Jabal al-Nour">Jabal al-Nour</a> where, according to Muslim belief, Muhammad received his first revelation</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rashid_al-Din_Tabib_-_Jami_al-Tawarikh,_f.45v_detail_-_c._1306-15.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4374" data-file-width="5877" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Rashid_al-Din_Tabib_-_Jami_al-Tawarikh%2C_f.45v_detail_-_c._1306-15.png/220px-Rashid_al-Din_Tabib_-_Jami_al-Tawarikh%2C_f.45v_detail_-_c._1306-15.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Rashid_al-Din_Tabib_-_Jami_al-Tawarikh%2C_f.45v_detail_-_c._1306-15.png/330px-Rashid_al-Din_Tabib_-_Jami_al-Tawarikh%2C_f.45v_detail_-_c._1306-15.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Rashid_al-Din_Tabib_-_Jami_al-Tawarikh%2C_f.45v_detail_-_c._1306-15.png/440px-Rashid_al-Din_Tabib_-_Jami_al-Tawarikh%2C_f.45v_detail_-_c._1306-15.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rashid_al-Din_Tabib_-_Jami_al-Tawarikh,_f.45v_detail_-_c._1306-15.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Muhammad receiving his first revelation from Gabriel in <i><a href="/wiki/Jami%27_al-tawarikh" title="Jami' al-tawarikh">Jami' al-tawarikh</a></i> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rashid-al-Din_Hamadani" title="Rashid-al-Din Hamadani">RashÄ«d al-DÄ«n á¹¬abÄ«b</a> (1307)</div></div></div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahih_Bukhari" title="Sahih Bukhari">Sahih Bukhari</a> narrates Muhammad describing his revelations as "sometimes it is (revealed) like the ringing of a bell". <a href="/wiki/Aisha" title="Aisha">Aisha</a> reported, "I saw the Prophet being inspired Divinely on a very cold day and noticed the sweat dropping from his forehead (as the Inspiration was over)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Alford_T._Welch" title="Alford T. Welch">Welch</a> these descriptions may be considered genuine, since they are unlikely to have been forged by later Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup> Muhammad was confident that he could distinguish his own thoughts from these messages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup> According to the Quran, one of the main roles of Muhammad is to warn the unbelievers of their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eschatological" title="Eschatological">eschatological</a> punishment (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quran_38:70" title="Quran 38:70">Quran 38:70</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quran_6:19" title="Quran 6:19">Quran 6:19</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> Occasionally the Quran did not explicitly refer to Judgment day but provided examples from the history of extinct communities and warns Muhammad's contemporaries of similar calamities ( Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/41?startingVerse=13" rel="nofollow">41:13â16</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoQ-Muhammad_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-EoQ-Muhammad-90">[88]</a></sup> Muhammad did not only warn those who rejected God's revelation, but also dispensed good news for those who abandoned evil, listening to the divine words and serving God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup> Muhammad's mission also involves preaching monotheism: The Quran commands Muhammad to proclaim and praise the name of his Lord and instructs him not to worship idols or associate other deities with God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoQ-Muhammad_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-EoQ-Muhammad-90">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>The key themes of the early Quranic verses included the responsibility of man towards his creator; the resurrection of the dead, God's final judgment followed by vivid descriptions of the tortures in Hell and pleasures in Paradise, and the signs of God in all aspects of life. Religious duties required of the believers at this time were few: belief in God, asking for forgiveness of sins, offering frequent prayers, assisting others particularly those in need, rejecting cheating and the love of wealth (considered to be significant in the commercial life of Mecca), being chaste and not committing <a href="/wiki/Women_in_pre-Islamic_Arabia#Female_infanticide" title="Women in pre-Islamic Arabia">female</a> <a href="/wiki/Infanticide" title="Infanticide">infanticide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition">Opposition</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Muslims_by_Meccans" title="Persecution of Muslims by Meccans">Persecution of Muslims by Meccans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Migration_to_Abyssinia" title="Migration to Abyssinia">Migration to Abyssinia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Surat_An-Najm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="693" data-file-width="924" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Surat_An-Najm.jpg/220px-Surat_An-Najm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Surat_An-Najm.jpg/330px-Surat_An-Najm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Surat_An-Najm.jpg/440px-Surat_An-Najm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Surat_An-Najm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The last verse from <a href="/wiki/An-Najm" title="An-Najm">An-Najm</a>: <i>"So prostrate to Allah and worship."</i> Muhammad's message of <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheism</a> challenged the traditional order</div></div></div>
<p>According to Muslim tradition, Muhammad's wife <a href="/wiki/Khadija_bint_Khuwaylid" title="Khadija bint Khuwaylid">Khadija</a> was the first to believe he was a prophet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt53-86_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watt53-86-92">[90]</a></sup> She was followed by Muhammad's ten-year-old cousin <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a>, close friend <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a>, and adopted son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zayd_ibn_Harithah" title="Zayd ibn Harithah">Zaid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt53-86_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watt53-86-92">[90]</a></sup> Around 613, Muhammad began to preach to the public ( Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/26?startingVerse=214" rel="nofollow">26:214</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al-A'zami2_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Al-A'zami2-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup> Most Meccans ignored and mocked him, though a few became his followers. There were three main groups of early converts to Islam: younger brothers and sons of great merchants; people who had fallen out of the first rank in their tribe or failed to attain it; and the weak, mostly unprotected foreigners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_1977_36_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge_1977_36-94">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Ibn Saad, opposition in Mecca started when Muhammad delivered verses that condemned idol worship and the polytheism practiced by the Meccan forefathers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup> However, the Quranic exegesis maintains that it began as Muhammad started public preaching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin-96">[94]</a></sup> As his followers increased, Muhammad became a threat to the local tribes and rulers of the city, whose wealth rested upon the Kaaba, the focal point of Meccan religious life that Muhammad threatened to overthrow. Muhammad's denunciation of the Meccan traditional religion was especially offensive to his own tribe, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quraysh_(tribe)" title="Quraysh (tribe)">Quraysh</a>, as they were the guardians of the Kaaba.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_1977_36_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge_1977_36-94">[92]</a></sup> Powerful merchants attempted to convince Muhammad to abandon his preaching; he was offered admission to the inner circle of merchants, as well as an advantageous marriage. He refused both of these offers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_1977_36_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge_1977_36-94">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25%; ; background-color: #ffeeaa;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p><i>Have We not made for him two eyes? And a tongue and two lips? And have shown him the two ways? But he has not broken through the difficult pass. And what can make you know what is the difficult pass? It is the freeing of a slave. Or feeding on a day of severe hunger; an orphan of near relationship, or a needy person in misery. And then being among those who believed and advised one another to patience and advised one another to mercy.</i>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">â Quran (90:8â17)</cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Tradition records at great length the persecution and ill-treatment towards Muhammad and his followers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sumayyah_bint_Khayyat" title="Sumayyah bint Khayyat">Sumayyah bint Khayyat</a>, a slave of a prominent Meccan leader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amr_ibn_Hish%C4%81m" title="Amr ibn HishÄm">Abu Jahl</a>, is famous as the first martyr of Islam; killed with a spear by her master when she refused to give up her faith. <a href="/wiki/Bilal_ibn_Rabah" title="Bilal ibn Rabah">Bilal</a>, another Muslim slave, was tortured by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umayyah_ibn_Khalaf" title="Umayyah ibn Khalaf">Umayyah ibn Khalaf</a> who placed a heavy rock on his chest to force his conversion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 615, some of Muhammad's followers <a href="/wiki/Migration_to_Abyssinia" title="Migration to Abyssinia">emigrated</a> to the Ethiopian <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aksum" title="Kingdom of Aksum">Kingdom of Aksum</a> and founded a small colony under the protection of the Christian Ethiopian emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A%E1%B9%A3%E1%B8%A5ama_ibn_Abjar" title="Aá¹£á¸¥ama ibn Abjar">Aá¹£á¸¥ama ibn Abjar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Sa%27d_al-Baghdadi" title="Ibn Sa'd al-Baghdadi">Ibn Sa'ad</a> mentions two separate migrations. According to him, most of the Muslims returned to Mecca prior to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegira" title="Hegira">Hijra</a>, while a second group rejoined them in Medina. <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hisham" title="Ibn Hisham">Ibn Hisham</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Jarir_al-Tabari" title="Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari">Tabari</a>, however, only talk about one migration to Ethiopia. These accounts agree that Meccan persecution played a major role in Muhammad's decision to suggest that a number of his followers seek refuge among the Christians in Abyssinia. According to the famous letter of <a href="/wiki/Urwah_ibn_Zubayr" title="Urwah ibn Zubayr">Ê¿Urwa</a> preserved in al-Tabari, the majority of Muslims returned to their native town as Islam gained strength and as high ranking Meccans, such as <a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Umar</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hamza_ibn_Abdul-Muttalib" title="Hamza ibn Abdul-Muttalib">Hamzah</a>, converted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, there is a completely different story on the reason why the Muslims returned from Ethiopia to Mecca. According to this accountâinitially mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Al-Waqidi" title="Al-Waqidi">Al-Waqidi</a> then rehashed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Sa%27d_al-Baghdadi" title="Ibn Sa'd al-Baghdadi">Ibn Sa'ad</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Jarir_al-Tabari" title="Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari">Tabari</a>, but not by <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hisham" title="Ibn Hisham">Ibn Hisham</a> and not by <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Ishaq" title="Ibn Ishaq">Ibn Ishaq</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup>âMuhammad, desperately hoping for an accommodation with his tribe, pronounced a verse acknowledging the existence of three Meccan goddesses considered to be the daughters of Allah. Muhammad retracted the verses the next day at the behest of Gabriel, claiming that the verses were whispered by the devil himself. Instead, a ridicule of these gods was offered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[n 3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[n 4]</a></sup> This episode, known as "The Story of the Cranes," is also known as "<a href="/wiki/Satanic_Verses" title="Satanic Verses">Satanic Verses</a>". According to the story, this led to a general reconciliation between Muhammad and the Meccans, and the Abyssinia Muslims began to return home. When they arrived Gabriel had informed Muhammad that the two verses were not part of the revelation, but had been inserted by Satan. Notable scholars at the time argued against the historic authenticity of these verses and the story itself on various grounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[n 5]</a></sup> Al-Waqidi was severely criticized by Islamic scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Malik_ibn_Anas" title="Malik ibn Anas">Malik ibn Anas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Shafi%27i" title="Al-Shafi'i">al-Shafi'i</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Hanbal" title="Ahmad ibn Hanbal">Ahmad ibn Hanbal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Nasa%27i" title="Al-Nasa'i">Al-Nasa'i</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Bukhari" title="Al-Bukhari">al-Bukhari</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Dawood" title="Abu Dawood">Abu Dawood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Nawawi" title="Al-Nawawi">Al-Nawawi</a> and others as a liar and forger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arafat_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-arafat-107">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[105]</a></sup> Later, the incident received some acceptance among certain groups, though strong objections to it continued onwards past the tenth century. The objections continued until rejection of these verses and the story itself eventually became the only acceptable orthodox Muslim position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 616 (or 617), the leaders of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Makhzum" title="Makhzum">Makhzum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Banu_Abd-Shams" title="Banu Abd-Shams">Banu Abd-Shams</a>, two important Quraysh clans, declared a public boycott against Banu Hashim, their commercial rival, to pressure it into withdrawing its protection of Muhammad. The boycott lasted three years but eventually collapsed as it failed in its objective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Momen_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Momen-113">[108]</a></sup> During this time, Muhammad was able to preach only during the holy pilgrimage months in which all hostilities between Arabs were suspended.
</p>
<h3><span id="Isra_and_Mi.27raj"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Isra_and_Mi'raj">Isra and Mi'raj</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isra_and_Mi%27raj" title="Isra and Mi'raj">Isra and Mi'raj</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dome_of_the_Rock,_from_Governor%27s_House,_Francis_Bedford_1862.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1428" data-file-width="1734" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Dome_of_the_Rock%2C_from_Governor%27s_House%2C_Francis_Bedford_1862.jpg/220px-Dome_of_the_Rock%2C_from_Governor%27s_House%2C_Francis_Bedford_1862.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Dome_of_the_Rock%2C_from_Governor%27s_House%2C_Francis_Bedford_1862.jpg/330px-Dome_of_the_Rock%2C_from_Governor%27s_House%2C_Francis_Bedford_1862.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Dome_of_the_Rock%2C_from_Governor%27s_House%2C_Francis_Bedford_1862.jpg/440px-Dome_of_the_Rock%2C_from_Governor%27s_House%2C_Francis_Bedford_1862.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dome_of_the_Rock,_from_Governor%27s_House,_Francis_Bedford_1862.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Temple_Mount" title="Temple Mount">Masjid Al-Aqsa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, also known as the Haram ash-Sharif or the Temple Mount, takes its name from the "farthest mosque" described in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Isra" title="Al-Isra">Surah 17</a>, where Muhammad travelled in his night journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grabar2006_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grabar2006-114">[109]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Islamic tradition states that in 620, Muhammad experienced the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isra_and_Mi%27raj" title="Isra and Mi'raj">Isra and Mi'raj</a></i>, a miraculous night-long journey said to have occurred with the angel <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">Gabriel</a>. At the journey's beginning, the <i>Isra</i>, he is said to have traveled from <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Buraq" title="Buraq">winged steed</a> to "the farthest mosque." Later, during the <i>Mi'raj</i>, Muhammad is said to have toured <a href="/wiki/Jannah" title="Jannah">heaven</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jahannam" title="Jahannam">hell</a>, and spoke with earlier prophets, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_Abraham" title="Islamic view of Abraham">Abraham</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_Moses" title="Islamic view of Moses">Moses</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">Jesus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoIMW_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-EoIMW-115">[110]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Ishaq" title="Ibn Ishaq">Ibn Ishaq</a>, author of the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sirah_Rasul_Allah" title="Sirah Rasul Allah">biography of Muhammad</a>, presents the event as a spiritual experience; later historians, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Jarir_al-Tabari" title="Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari">Al-Tabari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Kathir" title="Ibn Kathir">Ibn Kathir</a>, present it as a physical journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoIMW_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-EoIMW-115">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some western scholars<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2014)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> hold that the Isra and Mi'raj journey traveled through the heavens from the sacred enclosure at Mecca to the celestial <i>al-Baytu l-MaÊ¿mur</i> (heavenly prototype of the Kaaba); later traditions indicate Muhammad's journey as having been from Mecca to Jerusalem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[111]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2014)">pageÂ needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_years_before_Hijra">Last years before Hijra</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Domeoftherock1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Domeoftherock1.jpg/220px-Domeoftherock1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Domeoftherock1.jpg/330px-Domeoftherock1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Domeoftherock1.jpg/440px-Domeoftherock1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Domeoftherock1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Quranic inscriptions on the <a href="/wiki/Dome_of_the_Rock" title="Dome of the Rock">Dome of the Rock</a>. It marks the spot Muhammad is believed by Muslims to have ascended to <a href="/wiki/Jannah" title="Jannah">heaven</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BloomBlair2009_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-BloomBlair2009-117">[112]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Muhammad's wife Khadijah and uncle Abu Talib both died in 619, the year thus being known as the "<a href="/wiki/Year_of_Sorrow" title="Year of Sorrow">Year of Sorrow</a>". With the death of Abu Talib, leadership of the Banu Hashim clan passed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Lahab" title="Abu Lahab">Abu Lahab</a>, a tenacious enemy of Muhammad. Soon afterward, Abu Lahab withdrew the clan's protection over Muhammad. This placed Muhammad in danger; the withdrawal of clan protection implied that blood revenge for his killing would not be exacted. Muhammad then <a href="/wiki/Muhammad%27s_visit_to_Ta%27if" title="Muhammad's visit to Ta'if">visited Ta'if</a>, another important city in Arabia, and tried to find a protector, but his effort failed and further brought him into physical danger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Momen_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Momen-113">[108]</a></sup> Muhammad was forced to return to Mecca. A Meccan man named Mut'im ibn Adi (and the protection of the tribe of <a href="/wiki/Banu_Nawfal" title="Banu Nawfal">Banu Nawfal</a>) made it possible for him to safely re-enter his native city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-8"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Momen_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-Momen-113">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many people visited Mecca on business or as pilgrims to the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a>. Muhammad took this opportunity to look for a new home for himself and his followers. After several unsuccessful negotiations, he found hope with some men from Yathrib (later called Medina).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-9"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup> The Arab population of Yathrib were familiar with monotheism and were prepared for the appearance of a prophet because a Jewish community existed there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-10"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup> They also hoped, by the means of Muhammad and the new faith, to gain supremacy over Mecca; the Yathrib were jealous of its importance as the place of pilgrimage. Converts to Islam came from nearly all Arab tribes in Medina; by June of the subsequent year, seventy-five Muslims came to Mecca for pilgrimage and to meet Muhammad. Meeting him secretly by night, the group made what is known as the "<i><a href="/wiki/Second_pledge_at_al-Aqabah" title="Second pledge at al-Aqabah">Second Pledge of al-'Aqaba</a></i>", or, in Orientalists' view, the "<i>Pledge of War</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[113]</a></sup> Following the pledges at Aqabah, Muhammad encouraged his followers to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" title="Hijra (Islam)">emigrate</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yathrib" title="Yathrib">Yathrib</a>. As with the <a href="/wiki/Migration_to_Abyssinia" title="Migration to Abyssinia">migration to Abyssinia</a>, the Quraysh attempted to stop the emigration. However, almost all Muslims managed to leave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P87_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-P87-119">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hijra">Hijra</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegira" title="Hegira">Hegira</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Military_career_of_Muhammad" title="Military career of Muhammad">Military career of Muhammad</a></div>
<p>The Hijra is the migration of Muhammad and his followers from Mecca to Medina in 622 CE. In June 622, warned of a plot to assassinate him, Muhammad secretly slipped out of Mecca and moved his followers to Medina,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al-A'zami4_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Al-A'zami4-120">[115]</a></sup> 450 kilometres (280 miles) north of Mecca.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al-A'zami3_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Al-A'zami3-121">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
