<li><a href="/wiki/Third_Expedition_of_Wadi_al_Qura" title="Third Expedition of Wadi al Qura">3rd Wadi al-Qurra'</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mu%27tah" title="Battle of Mu'tah">Mu'tah</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_Mecca" title="Conquest of Mecca">Mecca</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hunayn" title="Battle of Hunayn">Hunayn</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expedition_to_Tabuk" title="Expedition to Tabuk">Tabuk</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Autas" title="Battle of Autas">Autas</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Ta%27if" title="Siege of Ta'if">Ta'if</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p>Following the emigration, the people of Mecca seized property of Muslim emigrants to Medina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[123]</a></sup> War would later break out between the people of Mecca and the Muslims. Muhammad delivered <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quranic</a> verses permitting Muslims to fight the Meccans (see sura <a href="/wiki/Al-Hajj" title="Al-Hajj">Al-Hajj</a>,  Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/22?startingVerse=39" rel="nofollow">22:39â40</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[124]</a></sup> According to the traditional account, on 11 February 624, while praying in the <a href="/wiki/Masjid_al-Qiblatayn" title="Masjid al-Qiblatayn">Masjid al-Qiblatayn</a> in Medina, Muhammad received revelations from God that he should be facing Mecca rather than Jerusalem during prayer. Muhammad adjusted to the new direction, and his companions praying with him followed his lead, beginning the tradition of facing Mecca during prayer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt1974_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watt1974-130">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25%; ; background-color: #ffeeaa;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p><i>Permission has been given to those who are being fought, because they were wronged. And indeed, Allah is competent to give them victory. Those who have been evicted from their homes without rightâonly because they say, "Our Lord is Allah." And were it not that Allah checks the people, some by means of others, there would have been demolished <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monasteries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Church_(building)" title="Church (building)">churches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Synagogue" title="Synagogue">synagogues</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosques</a> in which the name of Allah is much mentioned. And Allah will surely support those who support Him. Indeed, Allah is Powerful and Exalted in Might.</i>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">â Quran (22:39â40)</cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Muhammad ordered a number of raids to capture Meccan caravans, but only the 8th of them, the Raid of Nakhla, resulted in actual fighting and capture of booty and prisoners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-131">[126]</a></sup> In March 624, Muhammad led some three hundred warriors in a raid on a Meccan merchant caravan. The Muslims set an ambush for the caravan at Badr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[127]</a></sup> Aware of the plan, the Meccan caravan eluded the Muslims. A Meccan force was sent to protect the caravan and went on to confront the Muslims upon receiving word that the caravan was safe. The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Badr" title="Battle of Badr">Battle of Badr</a> commenced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[128]</a></sup> Though outnumbered more than three to one, the Muslims won the battle, killing at least forty-five Meccans with fourteen Muslims dead. They also succeeded in killing many Meccan leaders, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amr_ibn_Hish%C4%81m" title="Amr ibn HishÄm">Abu Jahl</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[129]</a></sup> Seventy prisoners had been acquired, many of whom were ransomed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewisw_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewisw-135">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W123_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-W123-136">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodinson_168-9_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rodinson_168-9-137">[132]</a></sup> Muhammad and his followers saw the victory as confirmation of their faith<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-15"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup> and Muhammad ascribed the victory to the assistance of an invisible host of angels. The Quranic verses of this period, unlike the Meccan verses, dealt with practical problems of government and issues like the distribution of spoils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>The victory strengthened Muhammad's position in Medina and dispelled earlier doubts among his followers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[134]</a></sup> As a result, the opposition to him became less vocal. Pagans who had not yet converted were very bitter about the advance of Islam. Two pagans, <a href="/wiki/Asma_bint_Marwan" title="Asma bint Marwan">Asma bint Marwan</a> of the Aws Manat tribe and <a href="/wiki/Abu_%27Afak" title="Abu 'Afak">Abu 'Afak</a> of the 'Amr b. 'Awf tribe, had composed verses taunting and insulting the Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watt-medina-178_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-watt-medina-178-140">[135]</a></sup> They were killed by people belonging to their own or related clans, and Muhammad did not disapprove of the killings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-watt-medina-178_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-watt-medina-178-140">[135]</a></sup> This report, however, is considered by some to be a fabrication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[136]</a></sup> Most members of those tribes converted to Islam, and little pagan opposition remained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Muhammad expelled from Medina the <a href="/wiki/Banu_Qaynuqa" title="Banu Qaynuqa">Banu Qaynuqa</a>, one of three main Jewish tribes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-16"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup> but some historians contend that the expulsion happened after Muhammad's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[138]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Al-Waqidi" title="Al-Waqidi">al-Waqidi</a>, after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abd-Allah_ibn_Ubaiy" title="Abd-Allah ibn Ubaiy">Abd-Allah ibn Ubaiy</a> spoke for them, Muhammad refrained from executing them and commanded that they be exiled from Medina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[139]</a></sup> Following the Battle of Badr, Muhammad also made mutual-aid alliances with a number of Bedouin tribes to protect his community from attacks from the northern part of <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-17"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflict_with_Mecca">Conflict with Mecca</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Uhud" title="Battle of Uhud">Battle of Uhud</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Prophet_Muhammad_and_the_Muslim_Army_at_the_Battle_of_Uhud,_from_the_Siyer-i_Nebi,_1595.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2767" data-file-width="1746" decoding="async" height="349" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/The_Prophet_Muhammad_and_the_Muslim_Army_at_the_Battle_of_Uhud%2C_from_the_Siyer-i_Nebi%2C_1595.jpg/220px-The_Prophet_Muhammad_and_the_Muslim_Army_at_the_Battle_of_Uhud%2C_from_the_Siyer-i_Nebi%2C_1595.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/The_Prophet_Muhammad_and_the_Muslim_Army_at_the_Battle_of_Uhud%2C_from_the_Siyer-i_Nebi%2C_1595.jpg/330px-The_Prophet_Muhammad_and_the_Muslim_Army_at_the_Battle_of_Uhud%2C_from_the_Siyer-i_Nebi%2C_1595.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/The_Prophet_Muhammad_and_the_Muslim_Army_at_the_Battle_of_Uhud%2C_from_the_Siyer-i_Nebi%2C_1595.jpg/440px-The_Prophet_Muhammad_and_the_Muslim_Army_at_the_Battle_of_Uhud%2C_from_the_Siyer-i_Nebi%2C_1595.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Prophet_Muhammad_and_the_Muslim_Army_at_the_Battle_of_Uhud,_from_the_Siyer-i_Nebi,_1595.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"The Prophet Muhammad and the Muslim Army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Uhud" title="Battle of Uhud">Battle of Uhud</a>", from a 1595 edition of the <a href="/wiki/Mamluk" title="Mamluk">Mamluk</a>-Turkic <i><a href="/wiki/Siyer-i_Nebi" title="Siyer-i Nebi">Siyer-i Nebi</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>The Meccans were eager to avenge their defeat. To maintain economic prosperity, the Meccans needed to restore their prestige, which had been reduced at Badr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[140]</a></sup> In the ensuing months, the Meccans sent ambush parties to Medina while Muhammad led expeditions against tribes allied with Mecca and sent raiders onto a Meccan caravan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[141]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Sufyan" title="Abu Sufyan">Abu Sufyan</a> gathered an army of 3000 men and set out for an attack on Medina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_1960_45_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis_1960_45-147">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>A scout alerted Muhammad of the Meccan army's presence and numbers a day later. The next morning, at the Muslim conference of war, a dispute arose over how best to repel the Meccans. Muhammad and many senior figures suggested it would be safer to fight within Medina and take advantage of the heavily fortified strongholds. Younger Muslims argued that the Meccans were destroying crops, and huddling in the strongholds would destroy Muslim prestige. Muhammad eventually conceded to the younger Muslims and readied the Muslim force for battle. Muhammad led his force outside to the mountain of Uhud (the location of the Meccan camp) and fought the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Uhud" title="Battle of Uhud">Battle of Uhud</a> on 23 March 625.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[144]</a></sup> Although the Muslim army had the advantage in early encounters, lack of discipline on the part of strategically placed archers led to a Muslim defeat; 75 Muslims were killed, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hamza_ibn_%E2%80%98Abd_al-Muttalib" title="Hamza ibn âAbd al-Muttalib">Hamza</a>, Muhammad's uncle who became one of the best known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martyrdom_in_Islam" title="Martyrdom in Islam">martyrs in the Muslim tradition</a>. The Meccans did not pursue the Muslims; instead, they marched back to Mecca declaring victory. The announcement is probably because Muhammad was wounded and thought dead. When they discovered that Muhammad lived, the Meccans did not return due to false information about new forces coming to his aid. The attack had failed to achieve their aim of completely destroying the Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[146]</a></sup> The Muslims buried the dead and returned to Medina that evening. Questions accumulated about the reasons for the loss; Muhammad delivered Quranic verses  <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/3?startingVerse=152" rel="nofollow">3:152</a> indicating that the defeat was twofold: partly a punishment for disobedience, partly a test for steadfastness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>Abu Sufyan directed his effort towards another attack on Medina. He gained support from the nomadic tribes to the north and east of Medina; using propaganda about Muhammad's weakness, promises of booty, memories of Quraysh prestige and through bribery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt_Medina_30_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watt_Medina_30-153">[148]</a></sup> Muhammad's new policy was to prevent alliances against him. Whenever alliances against Medina were formed, he sent out expeditions to break them up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt_Medina_30_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watt_Medina_30-153">[148]</a></sup> Muhammad heard of men massing with hostile intentions against Medina, and reacted in a severe manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[149]</a></sup> One example is the assassination of <a href="/wiki/Ka%27b_ibn_al-Ashraf" title="Ka'b ibn al-Ashraf">Ka'b ibn al-Ashraf</a>, a chieftain of the Jewish tribe of <a href="/wiki/Banu_Nadir" title="Banu Nadir">Banu Nadir</a>. Al-Ashraf went to Mecca and wrote poems that roused the Meccans' grief, anger and desire for revenge after the Battle of Badr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[151]</a></sup> Around a year later, Muhammad expelled the Banu Nadir from Medina<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[152]</a></sup> forcing their emigration to Syria; he allowed them to take some possessions, as he was unable to subdue the Banu Nadir in their strongholds. The rest of their property was claimed by Muhammad in the name of God as it was not gained with bloodshed. Muhammad surprised various Arab tribes, individually, with overwhelming force, causing his enemies to unite to annihilate him. Muhammad's attempts to prevent a confederation against him were unsuccessful, though he was able to increase his own forces and stopped many potential tribes from joining his enemies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_the_Trench">Battle of the Trench</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Trench" title="Battle of the Trench">Battle of the Trench</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Masjid_al-Qiblatain.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1491" data-file-width="1989" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Masjid_al-Qiblatain.jpg/220px-Masjid_al-Qiblatain.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Masjid_al-Qiblatain.jpg/330px-Masjid_al-Qiblatain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Masjid_al-Qiblatain.jpg/440px-Masjid_al-Qiblatain.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Masjid_al-Qiblatain.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Masjid_al-Qiblatayn" title="Masjid al-Qiblatayn">Masjid al-Qiblatayn</a>, where Muhammad established the new <a href="/wiki/Qibla" title="Qibla">Qibla</a>, or direction of prayer</div></div></div>
<p>With the help of the exiled <a href="/wiki/Banu_Nadir" title="Banu Nadir">Banu Nadir</a>, the Quraysh military leader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Sufyan" title="Abu Sufyan">Abu Sufyan</a> mustered a force of 10,000 men. Muhammad prepared a force of about 3,000 men and adopted a form of defense unknown in Arabia at that time; the Muslims dug a trench wherever Medina lay open to cavalry attack. The idea is credited to a Persian convert to Islam, <a href="/wiki/Salman_the_Persian" title="Salman the Persian">Salman the Persian</a>. The siege of Medina began on 31 March 627 and lasted two weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[154]</a></sup> Abu Sufyan's troops were unprepared for the fortifications, and after an ineffectual siege, the coalition decided to return home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[155]</a></sup> The Quran discusses this battle in sura Al-Ahzab, in verses  <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/33?startingVerse=9" rel="nofollow">33:9â27</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin-96">[94]</a></sup>
During the battle, the Jewish tribe of <a href="/wiki/Banu_Qurayza" title="Banu Qurayza">Banu Qurayza</a>, located to the south of Medina, entered into negotiations with Meccan forces to revolt against Muhammad. Although the Meccan forces were swayed by suggestions that Muhammad was sure to be overwhelmed, they desired reassurance in case the confederacy was unable to destroy him. No agreement was reached after prolonged negotiations, partly due to sabotage attempts by Muhammad's scouts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[156]</a></sup> After the coalition's retreat, the Muslims accused the Banu Qurayza of treachery and besieged them in their forts for 25 days. The Banu Qurayza eventually surrendered; according to <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Ishaq" title="Ibn Ishaq">Ibn Ishaq</a>, all the men apart from a few converts to Islam were beheaded, while the women and children were enslaved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[158]</a></sup> Walid N. Arafat and <a href="/wiki/Barakat_Ahmad" title="Barakat Ahmad">Barakat Ahmad</a> have disputed the accuracy of Ibn Ishaq's narrative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meri1_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meri1-164">[159]</a></sup> Arafat believes that Ibn Ishaq's Jewish sources, speaking over 100 years after the event, conflated this account with memories of earlier massacres in Jewish history; he notes that Ibn Ishaq was considered an unreliable historian by his contemporary <a href="/wiki/Malik_ibn_Anas" title="Malik ibn Anas">Malik ibn Anas</a>, and a transmitter of "odd tales" by the later <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hajar_al-Asqalani" title="Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani">Ibn Hajar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arafat_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arafat-165">[160]</a></sup> Ahmad argues that only some of the tribe were killed, while some of the fighters were merely enslaved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ahmad85_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmad85-166">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[162]</a></sup> Watt finds Arafat's arguments "not entirely convincing", while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meir_J._Kister" title="Meir J. Kister">Meir J. Kister</a> has contradicted<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (March 2009)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the arguments of Arafat and Ahmad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the siege of Medina, the Meccans exerted the available strength to destroy the Muslim community. The failure resulted in a significant loss of prestige; their trade with Syria vanished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[164]</a></sup> Following the Battle of the Trench, Muhammad made two expeditions to the north, both ended without any fighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-18"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup> While returning from one of these journeys (or some years earlier according to other early accounts), an <a href="/wiki/Aisha#Accusation_of_adultery" title="Aisha">accusation of adultery</a> was made against <a href="/wiki/Aisha" title="Aisha">Aisha</a>, Muhammad's wife. Aisha was exonerated from accusations when Muhammad announced he had received a revelation confirming Aisha's innocence and directing that charges of adultery be supported by four eyewitnesses (sura 24, <a href="/wiki/An-Nur" title="An-Nur">An-Nur</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt-encyc-online_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watt-encyc-online-170">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Truce_of_Hudaybiyyah">Truce of Hudaybiyyah</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Hudaybiyyah" title="Treaty of Hudaybiyyah">Treaty of Hudaybiyyah</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style=";">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>"In your name, O God!<br/>This is the treaty of peace between Muhammad Ibn Abdullah and Suhayl Ibn Amr. They have agreed to allow their arms to rest for ten years. During this time each party shall be secure, and neither shall injure the other; no secret damage shall be inflicted, but honesty and honour shall prevail between them. Whoever in Arabia wishes to enter into a treaty or covenant with Muhammad can do so, and whoever wishes to enter into a treaty or covenant with the Quraysh can do so. And if a Qurayshite comes without the permission of his guardian to Muhammad, he shall be delivered up to the Quraysh; but if, on the other hand, one of Muhammad's people comes to the Quraysh, he shall not be delivered up to Muhammad. This year, Muhammad, with his companions, must withdraw from Mecca, but next year, he may come to Mecca and remain for three days, yet without their weapons except those of a traveller; the swords remaining in their sheaths."
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">âThe statement of the treaty of Hudaybiyyah<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Text_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Text-171">[166]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div><p>
Although Muhammad had delivered Quranic verses commanding the <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[167]</a></sup> the Muslims had not performed it due to Quraysh enmity. In the month of <a href="/wiki/Shawwal" title="Shawwal">Shawwal</a> 628, Muhammad ordered his followers to obtain sacrificial animals and to prepare for a pilgrimage (<i><a href="/wiki/Umrah" title="Umrah">umrah</a></i>) to Mecca, saying that God had promised him the fulfillment of this goal in a vision when he was shaving his head after completion of the Hajj.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[168]</a></sup> Upon hearing of the approaching 1,400 Muslims, the Quraysh dispatched 200 cavalry to halt them. Muhammad evaded them by taking a more difficult route, enabling his followers to reach al-Hudaybiyya just outside Mecca.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hudaybiya_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hudaybiya-174">[169]</a></sup> According to Watt, although Muhammad's decision to make the pilgrimage was based on his dream, he was also demonstrating to the pagan Meccans that Islam did not threaten the prestige of the sanctuaries, that Islam was an Arabian religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hudaybiya_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hudaybiya-174">[169]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kaaba_Masjid_Haraam_Makkah.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Kaaba_Masjid_Haraam_Makkah.jpg/220px-Kaaba_Masjid_Haraam_Makkah.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Kaaba_Masjid_Haraam_Makkah.jpg/330px-Kaaba_Masjid_Haraam_Makkah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Kaaba_Masjid_Haraam_Makkah.jpg/440px-Kaaba_Masjid_Haraam_Makkah.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kaaba_Masjid_Haraam_Makkah.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> long held a major economic and religious role for the area. Seventeen months after Muhammad's arrival in Medina, it became the Muslim <a href="/wiki/Qibla" title="Qibla">Qibla</a>, or direction for prayer (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salat" title="Salat">salat</a>). The Kaaba has been rebuilt several times; the present structure, built in 1629, is a reconstruction of an earlier building dating to 683.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peters2005_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peters2005-175">[170]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Negotiations commenced with emissaries traveling to and from Mecca. While these continued, rumors spread that one of the Muslim negotiators, <a href="/wiki/Uthman" title="Uthman">Uthman bin al-Affan</a>, had been killed by the Quraysh. Muhammad called upon the pilgrims to make a pledge not to flee (or to stick with Muhammad, whatever decision he made) if the situation descended into war with Mecca. This pledge became known as the "Pledge of Acceptance" or the "<a href="/wiki/Pledge_of_the_Tree" title="Pledge of the Tree">Pledge under the Tree</a>". News of Uthman's safety allowed for negotiations to continue, and a treaty scheduled to last ten years was eventually signed between the Muslims and Quraysh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hudaybiya_174-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hudaybiya-174">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[171]</a></sup> The main points of the treaty included: cessation of hostilities, the deferral of Muhammad's pilgrimage to the following year, and agreement to send back any Meccan who emigrated to Medina without permission from their protector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hudaybiya_174-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hudaybiya-174">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many Muslims were not satisfied with the treaty. However, the Quranic sura "<a href="/wiki/Al-Fath" title="Al-Fath">Al-Fath</a>" (The Victory) (Quran  Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/48?startingVerse=1" rel="nofollow">48:1â29</a>) assured them that the expedition must be considered a victorious one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[172]</a></sup> It was later that Muhammad's followers realized the benefit behind the treaty. These benefits included the requirement of the Meccans to identify Muhammad as an equal, cessation of military activity allowing Medina to gain strength, and the admiration of Meccans who were impressed by the pilgrimage rituals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-19"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>After signing the truce, Muhammad assembled an expedition against the Jewish oasis of <a href="/wiki/Khaybar" title="Khaybar">Khaybar</a>, known as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Khaybar" title="Battle of Khaybar">Battle of Khaybar</a>. This was possibly due to housing the Banu Nadir who were inciting hostilities against Muhammad, or to regain prestige from what appeared as the inconclusive result of the truce of Hudaybiyya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_1960_45_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis_1960_45-147">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[173]</a></sup> According to Muslim tradition, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad%27s_letters_to_the_Heads-of-State" title="Muhammad's letters to the Heads-of-State">Muhammad also sent letters</a> to many rulers, asking them to convert to Islam (the exact date is given variously in the sources).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-20"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_Lings_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-King_Lings-179">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kings_Khan_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kings_Khan-180">[175]</a></sup> He sent messengers (with letters) to <a href="/wiki/Heraclius" title="Heraclius">Heraclius</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> (the eastern Roman Empire), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khosrau_II" title="Khosrau II">Khosrau</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">Persia</a>, the chief of <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a> and to some others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_Lings_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-King_Lings-179">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kings_Khan_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kings_Khan-180">[175]</a></sup> In the years following the truce of Hudaybiyya, Muhammad directed his forces against the Arabs on <a href="/wiki/Transjordan_(region)" title="Transjordan (region)">Transjordanian</a> Byzantine soil in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mu%27tah" title="Battle of Mu'tah">Battle of Mu'tah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[176]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years">Final years</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Conquest_of_Mecca">Conquest of Mecca</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_Mecca" title="Conquest of Mecca">Conquest of Mecca</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_after_the_conquest_of_Mecca" title="Muhammad after the conquest of Mecca">Muhammad after the conquest of Mecca</a></div>
<div class="depiction">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Siyer-i_Nebi_298a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="777" data-file-width="497" decoding="async" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Siyer-i_Nebi_298a.jpg/170px-Siyer-i_Nebi_298a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Siyer-i_Nebi_298a.jpg/255px-Siyer-i_Nebi_298a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Siyer-i_Nebi_298a.jpg/340px-Siyer-i_Nebi_298a.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Siyer-i_Nebi_298a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A depiction of Muhammad (with veiled face) advancing on Mecca from <a href="/wiki/Siyer-i_Nebi" title="Siyer-i Nebi">Siyer-i Nebi</a>, a 16th-century <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> manuscript. The angels Gabriel, Michael, Israfil and Azrail, are also shown.</div></div></div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Hudaybiyyah" title="Treaty of Hudaybiyyah">truce of Hudaybiyyah</a> was enforced for two years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-khan_274_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-khan_274-182">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lings_291_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lings_291-183">[178]</a></sup> The tribe of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banu_Khuza%27a" title="Banu Khuza'a">Banu Khuza'a</a> had good relations with Muhammad, whereas their enemies, the <a href="/wiki/Banu_Bakr_ibn_Abd_Manat" title="Banu Bakr ibn Abd Manat">Banu Bakr</a>, had allied with the Meccans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-khan_274_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-khan_274-182">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lings_291_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lings_291-183">[178]</a></sup> A clan of the Bakr made a night raid against the Khuza'a, killing a few of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-khan_274_182-2"><a href="#cite_note-khan_274-182">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lings_291_183-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lings_291-183">[178]</a></sup> The Meccans helped the Banu Bakr with weapons and, according to some sources, a few Meccans also took part in the fighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-khan_274_182-3"><a href="#cite_note-khan_274-182">[177]</a></sup> After this event, Muhammad sent a message to Mecca with three conditions, asking them to accept one of them. These were: either the Meccans would pay <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_money_(term)" title="Blood money (term)">blood money</a> for the slain among the Khuza'ah tribe, they disavow themselves of the Banu Bakr, or they should declare the truce of Hudaybiyyah null.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-khan_274_275_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-khan_274_275-184">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Meccans replied that they accepted the last condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-khan_274_275_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-khan_274_275-184">[179]</a></sup> Soon they realized their mistake and sent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Sufyan" title="Abu Sufyan">Abu Sufyan</a> to renew the Hudaybiyyah treaty, a request that was declined by Muhammad.
</p><p>Muhammad began to prepare for a campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[180]</a></sup> In 630, Muhammad marched on Mecca with 10,000 Muslim converts. With minimal casualties, Muhammad seized control of Mecca.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[181]</a></sup> He declared an amnesty for past offences, except for ten men and women who were "guilty of murder or other offences or had sparked off the war and disrupted the peace".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Subhani_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Subhani-187">[182]</a></sup> Some of these were later pardoned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[183]</a></sup> Most Meccans converted to Islam and Muhammad proceeded to destroy all the statues of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Islamic_Arabian_gods" title="Pre-Islamic Arabian gods">Arabian gods</a> in and around the Kaaba.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[185]</a></sup> According to reports collected by <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Ishaq" title="Ibn Ishaq">Ibn Ishaq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Azraqi" title="Al-Azraqi">al-Azraqi</a>, Muhammad personally spared paintings or frescos of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary_(mother_of_Jesus)" title="Mary (mother of Jesus)">Mary</a> and Jesus, but other traditions suggest that all pictures were erased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[186]</a></sup> The Quran discusses the conquest of Mecca.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin-96">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Conquest_of_Arabia">Conquest of Arabia</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hunayn" title="Battle of Hunayn">Battle of Hunayn</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expedition_to_Tabouk" title="Expedition to Tabouk">Expedition to Tabouk</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Muslim_Conquest.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="893" data-file-width="1006" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Muslim_Conquest.PNG/220px-Muslim_Conquest.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Muslim_Conquest.PNG/330px-Muslim_Conquest.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Muslim_Conquest.PNG/440px-Muslim_Conquest.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Muslim_Conquest.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Conquests of Muhammad (green lines) and the Rashidun caliphs (black lines). Shown: Byzantine empire (North and West) &amp; Sassanid-Persian empire (Northeast).</div></div></div>
<p>Following the conquest of Mecca, Muhammad was alarmed by a military threat from the confederate tribes of <a href="/wiki/Hawazin" title="Hawazin">Hawazin</a> who were raising an army double the size of Muhammad's. The <a href="/wiki/Hawazin" title="Hawazin">Banu Hawazin</a> were old enemies of the Meccans. They were joined by the <a href="/wiki/Banu_Thaqif" title="Banu Thaqif">Banu Thaqif</a> (inhabiting the city of Ta'if) who adopted an anti-Meccan policy due to the decline of the prestige of Meccans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[188]</a></sup> Muhammad defeated the Hawazin and Thaqif tribes in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hunayn" title="Battle of Hunayn">Battle of Hunayn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-21"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the same year, Muhammad organized an attack against northern Arabia because of their previous defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mu%27tah" title="Battle of Mu'tah">Battle of Mu'tah</a> and reports of hostility adopted against Muslims. With great difficulty he assembled 30,000 men; half of whom on the second day returned with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abd-Allah_ibn_Ubayy" title="Abd-Allah ibn Ubayy">Abd-Allah ibn Ubayy</a>, untroubled by the damning verses which Muhammad hurled at them. Although Muhammad did not engage with hostile forces at Tabuk, he received the submission of some local chiefs of the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-22"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>He also ordered the destruction of any remaining pagan idols in Eastern Arabia. The last city to hold out against the Muslims in Western Arabia was <a href="/wiki/Taif" title="Taif">Taif</a>. Muhammad refused to accept the city's surrender until they agreed to convert to Islam and allowed men to destroy the statue of their goddess <a href="/wiki/Al-Lat" title="Al-Lat">Al-Lat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-131">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[191]</a></sup>
</p><p>A year after the Battle of Tabuk, the Banu Thaqif sent emissaries to surrender to Muhammad and adopt Islam. Many bedouins submitted to Muhammad to safeguard against his attacks and to benefit from the spoils of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-23"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup> However, the bedouins were alien to the system of Islam and wanted to maintain independence: namely their code of virtue and ancestral traditions. Muhammad required a military and political agreement according to which they "acknowledge the suzerainty of Medina, to refrain from attack on the Muslims and their allies, and to pay the <a href="/wiki/Zakat" title="Zakat">Zakat</a>, the Muslim religious levy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Farewell_pilgrimage">Farewell pilgrimage</span></h4>
<div class="depiction">
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Farewell_Pilgrimage" title="Farewell Pilgrimage">Farewell Pilgrimage</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_event_of_Ghadir_Khumm" title="The event of Ghadir Khumm">The event of Ghadir Khumm</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maome.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Maome.jpg/220px-Maome.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Maome.jpg/330px-Maome.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Maome.jpg/440px-Maome.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maome.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anonymous illustration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Rayhan_Biruni" title="Abu Rayhan Biruni">al-BÄ«rÅ«nÄ«</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Remaining_Signs_of_Past_Centuries" title="The Remaining Signs of Past Centuries">The Remaining Signs of Past Centuries</a></i>, depicting Muhammad <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar#Prohibiting_NasÄ«â" title="Islamic calendar">prohibiting NasÄ«â</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Farewell_Pilgrimage" title="Farewell Pilgrimage">Farewell Pilgrimage</a>, 17th-century Ottoman copy of a 14th-century (<a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a>) manuscript (Edinburgh codex).</div></div></div>
</div>
<p>In 632, at the end of the tenth year after migration to Medina, Muhammad completed his first true Islamic pilgrimage, setting precedent for the annual Great Pilgrimage, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-24"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup> On the 9th of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhu_al-Hijjah" title="Dhu al-Hijjah">Dhu al-Hijjah</a> Muhammad delivered his <a href="/wiki/Farewell_Sermon" title="Farewell Sermon">Farewell Sermon</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Arafat" title="Mount Arafat">Mount Arafat</a> east of Mecca. In this sermon, Muhammad advised his followers not to follow certain pre-Islamic customs. For instance, he said a white has no superiority over a black, nor a black any superiority over a white except by piety and good action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[193]</a></sup> He abolished old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_feud" title="Blood feud">blood feuds</a> and disputes based on the former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tribal" title="Tribal">tribal</a> system and asked for old pledges to be returned as implications of the creation of the new Islamic community. Commenting on the vulnerability of women in his society, Muhammad asked his male followers to "be good to women, for they are powerless captives (<i>awan</i>) in your households. You took them in God's trust, and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_marriage_contract" title="Islamic marriage contract">legitimated your sexual relations</a> with the Word of God, so come to your senses people, and hear my words ..." He told them that they were entitled to discipline their wives but should do so with kindness. He addressed the issue of inheritance by forbidding false claims of paternity or of a client relationship to the deceased and forbade his followers to leave their wealth to a testamentary heir. He also upheld the sacredness of four lunar months in each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[194]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[195]</a></sup> According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> <a href="/wiki/Tafsir" title="Tafsir">tafsir</a>, the following Quranic verse was delivered during this event: "Today I have perfected your religion, and completed my favours for you and chosen Islam as a religion for you" (Quran  Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/5?startingVerse=3" rel="nofollow">5:3</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-25"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup> According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shia" title="Shia">Shia</a> tafsir, it refers to the appointment of <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Rabigh#Ghadir_Al-Khumm" title="Rabigh">pond of Khumm</a> as <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_Muhammad" title="Succession to Muhammad">Muhammad's successor</a>, this occurring a few days later when Muslims were returning from Mecca to Medina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[196]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_tomb">Death and tomb</span></h4><p>
A few months after the farewell pilgrimage, Muhammad fell ill and suffered for several days with fever, head pain, and weakness. He died on Monday, 8 June 632, in Medina, at the age of 62 or 63, in the house of his wife Aisha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USN&amp;WR_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-USN&amp;WR-202">[197]</a></sup> With his head resting on Aisha's lap, he asked her to dispose of his last worldly goods (seven coins), then spoke his final words: <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><i>O Allah, to Ar-Rafiq Al-A'la</i> (exalted friend, highest Friend or the uppermost, highest Friend in heaven).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[200]</a></sup></p><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite>Muhammad</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclopaedia_of_Islam" title="Encyclopaedia of Islam">Encyclopaedia of Islam</a></i>, Muhammad's death may be presumed to have been caused by Medinan fever exacerbated by physical and mental fatigue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-death-cause_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-death-cause-206">[201]</a></sup> Academics ReÅit Haylamaz and Fatih Harpci say that <i>Ar-Rafiq Al-A'la</i> is referring to God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[202]</a></sup>
</p><p>Muhammad was buried where he died in Aisha's house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-26"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peters90_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peters90-209">[204]</a></sup> During the reign of the Umayyad caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Walid_I" title="Al-Walid I">al-Walid I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Masjid_an-Nabawi" title="Al-Masjid an-Nabawi">al-Masjid an-Nabawi</a> (the Mosque of the Prophet) was expanded to include the site of <a href="/wiki/Al-Masjid_an-Nabawi" title="Al-Masjid an-Nabawi">Muhammad's tomb.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[205]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Green_Dome" title="Green Dome">Green Dome</a> above the tomb was built by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate_(Cairo)" title="Mamluk Sultanate (Cairo)">Mamluk</a> sultan <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al_Mansur_Qalawun" title="Al Mansur Qalawun">Al Mansur Qalawun</a> in the 13th century, although the green color was added in the 16th century, under the reign of <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> sultan <a href="/wiki/Suleiman_the_Magnificent" title="Suleiman the Magnificent">Suleiman the Magnificent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[206]</a></sup> Among tombs adjacent to that of Muhammad are those of his companions (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahabah" title="Sahabah">Sahabah</a>), the first two Muslim caliphs <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Umar</a>, and an empty one that <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam#Second_Coming" title="Jesus in Islam">Muslims believe awaits Jesus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peters90_209-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peters90-209">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al-HaqqaniKabbani2002_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-Al-HaqqaniKabbani2002-213">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>When <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saud_bin_Abdul-Aziz_bin_Muhammad_bin_Saud" title="Saud bin Abdul-Aziz bin Muhammad bin Saud">Saud bin Abdul-Aziz</a> took Medina in 1805, Muhammad's tomb was stripped of its gold and jewel ornamentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weston2008_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weston2008-214">[209]</a></sup> Adherents to <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabism</a>, Saud's followers, destroyed nearly every tomb dome in Medina in order to prevent their veneration,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weston2008_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weston2008-214">[209]</a></sup> and the one of Muhammad is reported to have narrowly escaped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Behrens-AbouseifVernoit2006_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-Behrens-AbouseifVernoit2006-215">[210]</a></sup> Similar events took place in 1925, when the <a href="/wiki/Ikhwan" title="Ikhwan">Saudi militias</a> retookâand this time managed to keepâthe city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weston2008b_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weston2008b-216">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cornell2007_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cornell2007-217">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ernst2004_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ernst2004-218">[213]</a></sup> In the Wahhabi interpretation of Islam, burial is to take place in unmarked graves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Behrens-AbouseifVernoit2006_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-Behrens-AbouseifVernoit2006-215">[210]</a></sup> Although the practice is frowned upon by the Saudis, many pilgrims continue to practice a <a href="/wiki/Ziyarat" title="Ziyarat">ziyarat</a>âa ritual visitâto the tomb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennett1998_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bennett1998-219">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark2011_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clark2011-220">[215]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:left;max-width:808px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Madina_Haram_at_evening.jpg" title="Al-Masjid an-Nabawi (&quot;the Prophet's mosque&quot;) in Medina, Saudi Arabia, with the Green Dome built over Muhammad's tomb in the center"><img alt="" data-file-height="475" data-file-width="2853" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Madina_Haram_at_evening.jpg/800px-Madina_Haram_at_evening.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Madina_Haram_at_evening.jpg/1200px-Madina_Haram_at_evening.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Madina_Haram_at_evening.jpg/1600px-Madina_Haram_at_evening.jpg 2x" width="800"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Madina_Haram_at_evening.jpg" title="File:Madina Haram at evening.jpg"> </a></div><a href="/wiki/Al-Masjid_an-Nabawi" title="Al-Masjid an-Nabawi">Al-Masjid an-Nabawi</a> ("the Prophet's mosque") in <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>, Saudi Arabia, with the <a href="/wiki/Green_Dome" title="Green Dome">Green Dome</a> built over Muhammad's tomb in the center</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_Muhammad">After Muhammad</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_Muhammad" title="Succession to Muhammad">Succession to Muhammad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rashidun" title="Rashidun">Rashidun</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Muslim conquests</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/220px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/330px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/440px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Expansion of the caliphate, 622â750Â CE. <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#a1584e; color:white;">Â </span>Â  Muhammad, 622â632Â CE.</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ef9070; color:black;">Â </span>Â  Rashidun caliphate, 632â661Â CE.</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#fad07d; color:black;">Â </span>Â  Umayyad caliphate, 661â750Â CE.</div></div></div></div>
<p>Muhammad united several of the <a href="/wiki/Tribes_of_Arabia" title="Tribes of Arabia">tribes of Arabia</a> into a single Arab Muslim religious polity in the last years of his life. With Muhammad's death, disagreement broke out over who his successor would be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lapidus_2002_pp_0_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lapidus_2002_pp_0-20">[18]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umar_ibn_al-Khattab" title="Umar ibn al-Khattab">Umar ibn al-Khattab</a>, a prominent companion of Muhammad, nominated <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a>, Muhammad's friend and collaborator. With additional support Abu Bakr was confirmed as the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">caliph</a>. This choice was disputed by some of Muhammad's companions, who held that Ali ibn Abi Talib, his cousin and son-in-law, had been designated the successor by Muhammad at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Event_of_Ghadir_Khumm" title="Event of Ghadir Khumm">Ghadir Khumm</a>. Abu Bakr immediately moved to strike against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a>) forces because of the previous defeat, although he first had to put down a rebellion by Arab tribes in an event that Muslim historians later referred to as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ridda_wars" title="Ridda wars">Ridda wars</a>, or "Wars of Apostasy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[216]</a></sup>
</p><p>The pre-Islamic Middle East was dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanian</a> empires. The <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Persian_Wars" title="RomanâPersian Wars">RomanâPersian Wars</a> between the two had devastated the region, making the empires unpopular amongst local tribes. Furthermore, in the lands that would be conquered by Muslims many Christians (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian_Church" title="Nestorian Church">Nestorians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophysite" title="Monophysite">Monophysites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Jacobites</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copt" title="Copt">Copts</a>) were disaffected from the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> which deemed them heretics. Within a decade Muslims conquered <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Syria" title="Muslim conquest of Syria">Byzantine Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Muslim conquest of Egypt">Byzantine Egypt</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[217]</a></sup> large parts of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">Persia</a>, and established the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_social_reforms">Islamic social reforms</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_social_changes_under_Islam" title="Early social changes under Islam">Early social changes under Islam</a></div>
<p>According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Montgomery_Watt" title="William Montgomery Watt">William Montgomery Watt</a>, religion for Muhammad was not a private and individual matter but "the total response of his personality to the total situation in which he found himself. He was responding [not only]... to the religious and intellectual aspects of the situation but also to the economic, social, and political pressures to which contemporary Mecca was subject."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[218]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Lewis" title="Bernard Lewis">Bernard Lewis</a> says there are two important political traditions in IslamâMuhammad as a statesman in Medina, and Muhammad as a rebel in Mecca. In his view, Islam is a great change, akin to a revolution, when introduced to new societies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LewisNYRB_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-LewisNYRB-224">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historians generally agree that Islamic social changes in areas such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_security" title="Social security">social security</a>, family structure, slavery and the rights of women and children improved on the <i>status quo</i> of Arab society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LewisNYRB_224-1"><a href="#cite_note-LewisNYRB-224">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[220]</a></sup> For example, according to Lewis, Islam "from the first denounced <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy_(class)" title="Aristocracy (class)">aristocratic</a> privilege, rejected hierarchy, and adopted a formula of the career open to the talents".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (August 2014)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LewisNYRB_224-2"><a href="#cite_note-LewisNYRB-224">[219]</a></sup> Muhammad's message transformed society and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_ethics" title="Islamic ethics">moral orders</a> of life in the Arabian Peninsula; society focused on the changes to perceived identity, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_view" title="World view">world view</a>, and the hierarchy of values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[221]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2014)">pageÂ needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
Economic reforms addressed the plight of the poor, which was becoming an issue in <a href="/wiki/Jahiliyyah" title="Jahiliyyah">pre-Islamic</a> Mecca.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[222]</a></sup> The Quran requires payment of an alms tax (<a href="/wiki/Zakat" title="Zakat">zakat</a>) for the benefit of the poor; as Muhammad's power grew he demanded that tribes who wished to ally with him implement the zakat in particular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[224]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Appearance">Appearance</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hilye-i_serif_5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="442" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Hilye-i_serif_5.jpg/220px-Hilye-i_serif_5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Hilye-i_serif_5.jpg/330px-Hilye-i_serif_5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Hilye-i_serif_5.jpg/440px-Hilye-i_serif_5.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hilye-i_serif_5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <i><a href="/wiki/Hilya" title="Hilya">hilya</a></i> containing a description of Muhammad, by <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligrapher</a> <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A2fiz_Osman" title="HÃ¢fiz Osman">HÃ¢fiz Osman</a> (1642â1698)</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Bukhari" title="Muhammad al-Bukhari">Muhammad al-Bukhari</a>'s book <a href="/wiki/Sahih_al-Bukhari" title="Sahih al-Bukhari">Sahih al-Bukhari</a>, in Chapter 61, Hadith 57 &amp; Hadith 60,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[226]</a></sup> Muhammad is depicted by two of his companions thus:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>God's Messenger was neither very tall nor short, neither absolutely white nor deep brown. His hair was neither curly nor lank. God sent him (as a Messenger) when he was forty years old. Afterwards he resided in Mecca for ten years and in Medina for ten more years. When God took him unto Him, there was scarcely twenty white hairs in his head and beard.</p><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite>Anas</cite></div></blockquote>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The Prophet was of moderate height having broad shoulders (long) hair reaching his ear-lobes. Once I saw him in a red cloak and I had never seen anyone more handsome than him.</p><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite>Al-Bara</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>The description given in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Isa_at-Tirmidhi" title="Muhammad ibn Isa at-Tirmidhi">Muhammad ibn Isa at-Tirmidhi</a>'s book <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shama%27il_Muhammadiyah" title="Shama'il Muhammadiyah">Shama'il al-Mustafa</a>, attributed to <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a> and Hind ibn Abi Hala is as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AsaniAbdel-Malek1995_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-AsaniAbdel-Malek1995-232">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schimmel1985_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schimmel1985-233">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[229]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Muhammad was middle-sized, did not have lank or crisp hair, was not fat, had a white circular face, wide black eyes, and long eye-lashes. When he walked, he walked as though he went down a <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/declivity" title="wikt:declivity">declivity</a>. He had the "seal of prophecy" between his shoulder blades ... He was bulky. His face shone like the moon. He was taller than middling stature but shorter than conspicuous tallness. He had thick, curly hair. The plaits of his hair were parted. His hair reached beyond the lobe of his ear. His complexion was <i>azhar</i> [bright, luminous]. Muhammad had a wide forehead, and fine, long, arched eyebrows which did not meet. Between his eyebrows there was a vein which distended when he was angry. The upper part of his nose was hooked; he was thick bearded, had smooth cheeks, a strong mouth, and his teeth were set apart. He had thin hair on his chest. His neck was like the neck of an ivory statue, with the purity of silver. Muhammad was proportionate, stout, firm-gripped, even of belly and chest, broad-chested and broad-shouldered.</p></blockquote>
<p>The "seal of prophecy" between Muhammad's shoulders is generally described as having been a type of raised mole the size of a pigeon's egg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schimmel1985_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schimmel1985-233">[228]</a></sup> Another description of Muhammad was provided by <a href="/wiki/Umm_Ma%27bad" title="Umm Ma'bad">Umm Ma'bad</a>, a woman he met on his journey to Medina:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Safi2009_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-Safi2009-235">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[231]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I saw a man, pure and clean, with a handsome face and a fine figure. He was not marred by a skinny body, nor was he overly small in the head and neck. He was graceful and elegant, with intensely black eyes and thick eyelashes. There was a huskiness in his voice, and his neck was long. His beard was thick, and his eyebrows were finely arched and joined together.
When silent, he was grave and dignified, and when he spoke, glory rose up and overcame him. He was from afar the most beautiful of men and the most glorious, and close up he was the sweetest and the loveliest. He was sweet of speech and articulate, but not petty or trifling. His speech was a string of cascading pearls, measured so that none despaired of its length, and no eye challenged him because of brevity. In company he is like a branch between two other branches, but he is the most flourishing of the three in appearance, and the loveliest in power. He has friends surrounding him, who listen to his words. If he commands, they obey implicitly, with eagerness and haste, without frown or complaint.</p></blockquote>
<p>Descriptions like these were often reproduced in calligraphic panels (Turkish: <i><a href="/wiki/Hilya" title="Hilya">hilye</a></i>), which in the 17th century developed into an art form of their own in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Safi2009_235-1"><a href="#cite_note-Safi2009-235">[230]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Household"><span class="anchor" id="household"></span>Household</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad%27s_wives" title="Muhammad's wives">Muhammad's wives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Bayt" title="Ahl al-Bayt">Ahl al-Bayt</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mrs_Aisha_room.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Mrs_Aisha_room.jpg/220px-Mrs_Aisha_room.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Mrs_Aisha_room.jpg/330px-Mrs_Aisha_room.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Mrs_Aisha_room.jpg/440px-Mrs_Aisha_room.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mrs_Aisha_room.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The tomb of Muhammad is located in the quarters of his third wife, <a href="/wiki/Aisha" title="Aisha">Aisha</a>. (<a href="/wiki/Al-Masjid_an-Nabawi" title="Al-Masjid an-Nabawi">Al-Masjid an-Nabawi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>Muhammad's life is traditionally defined into two periods: <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Mecca" title="Muhammad in Mecca">pre-hijra (emigration) in Mecca</a> (from 570 to 622), and <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Medina" title="Muhammad in Medina">post-hijra in Medina</a> (from 622 until 632). Muhammad is said to have had thirteen wives in total (although two have ambiguous accounts, <a href="/wiki/Rayhana_bint_Zayd" title="Rayhana bint Zayd">Rayhana bint Zayd</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maria_al-Qibtiyya" title="Maria al-Qibtiyya">Maria al-Qibtiyya</a>, as wife or concubine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barbara_Freyer_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barbara_Freyer-238">[233]</a></sup>). Eleven of the thirteen marriages occurred after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" title="Hijra (Islam)">migration to Medina</a>.
</p><p>At the age of 25, Muhammad married the wealthy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khadijah_bint_Khuwaylid" title="Khadijah bint Khuwaylid">Khadijah bint Khuwaylid</a> who was 40 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[234]</a></sup> The marriage lasted for 25Â years and was a happy one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esp2_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-Esp2-240">[235]</a></sup> Muhammad did not enter into marriage with another woman during this marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bullough_1998_119_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bullough_1998_119-241">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reeves46_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reeves46-242">[237]</a></sup> After Khadijah's death, Khawla bint Hakim suggested to Muhammad that he should marry <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sawda_bint_Zama" title="Sawda bint Zama">Sawda bint Zama</a>, a Muslim widow, or <a href="/wiki/Aisha" title="Aisha">Aisha</a>, daughter of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Um_Ruman" title="Um Ruman">Um Ruman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>. Muhammad is said to have asked for arrangements to marry both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt-encyc-online_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-Watt-encyc-online-170">[165]</a></sup> Muhammad's marriages after the death of Khadijah were contracted mostly for political or humanitarian reasons. The women were either widows of Muslims killed in battle and had been left without a protector, or belonged to important families or clans with whom it was necessary to honor and strengthen alliances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[238]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to traditional sources, Aisha was six or seven years old when betrothed to Muhammad,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt-encyc-online_170-2"><a href="#cite_note-Watt-encyc-online-170">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spellberg_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spellberg-244">[239]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Armstrong_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-Armstrong-245">[240]</a></sup> with the marriage not being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consummate" title="Consummate">consummated</a> until she reached the age of nine or ten years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[248]</a></sup> She was therefore a virgin at marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spellberg_244-2"><a href="#cite_note-Spellberg-244">[239]</a></sup> Modern Muslim authors who calculate Aisha's age based on other sources of information, such as a hadith about the age difference between Aisha and her sister Asma, estimate that she was over thirteen and perhaps in her late teens at the time of her marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[254]</a></sup>
</p><p>After migration to Medina, Muhammad, who was then in his fifties, married several more women.
</p><p>Muhammad performed household chores such as preparing food, sewing clothes, and repairing shoes. He is also said to have had accustomed his wives to dialogue; he listened to their advice, and the wives debated and even argued with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[255]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[256]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[257]</a></sup>
</p><p>Khadijah is said to have had four daughters with Muhammad (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruqayyah_bint_Muhammad" title="Ruqayyah bint Muhammad">Ruqayyah bint Muhammad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umm_Kulthum_bint_Muhammad" title="Umm Kulthum bint Muhammad">Umm Kulthum bint Muhammad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zainab_bint_Muhammad" title="Zainab bint Muhammad">Zainab bint Muhammad</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimah" title="Fatimah">Fatimah Zahra</a>) and two sons (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abd-Allah_ibn_Muhammad" title="Abd-Allah ibn Muhammad">Abd-Allah ibn Muhammad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qasim_ibn_Muhammad" title="Qasim ibn Muhammad">Qasim ibn Muhammad</a>, who both died in childhood). All but one of his daughters, Fatimah, died before him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nich_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nich-263">[258]</a></sup> Some Shi'a scholars contend that Fatimah was Muhammad's only daughter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[259]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Maria_al-Qibtiyya" title="Maria al-Qibtiyya">Maria al-Qibtiyya</a> bore him a son named <a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_ibn_Muhammad" title="Ibrahim ibn Muhammad">Ibrahim ibn Muhammad</a>, but the child died when he was two years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nich_263-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nich-263">[258]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nine of Muhammad's wives survived him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barbara_Freyer_238-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barbara_Freyer-238">[233]</a></sup> Aisha, who became known as Muhammad's favourite wife in Sunni tradition, survived him by decades and was instrumental in helping assemble the scattered sayings of Muhammad that form the Hadith literature for the Sunni branch of Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt-encyc-online_170-4"><a href="#cite_note-Watt-encyc-online-170">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>Muhammad's descendants through Fatimah are known as <i><a href="/wiki/Sharif" title="Sharif">sharifs</a></i>, <i>syeds</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Sayyid" title="Sayyid">sayyids</a></i>. These are honorific titles in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, <i>sharif</i> meaning 'noble' and <i>sayed</i> or <i>sayyid</i> meaning 'lord' or 'sir'. As Muhammad's only descendants, they are respected by both Sunni and Shi'a, though the Shi'a place much more emphasis and value on their distinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[260]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zayd_ibn_Harithah" title="Zayd ibn Harithah">Zayd ibn Haritha</a> was a slave that Muhammad bought, freed, and then adopted as his son. He also had a <a href="/wiki/Wet_nurse" title="Wet nurse">wetnurse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zad116_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zad116-266">[261]</a></sup> According to a BBC summary, "the Prophet Muhammad did not try to abolish slavery, and bought, sold, captured, and owned slaves himself. But he insisted that slave owners treat their slaves well and stressed the virtue of freeing slaves. Muhammad treated slaves as human beings and clearly held some in the highest esteem".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[262]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_tradition">Islamic tradition</span></h3>
