<li><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="1911" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/69/P_vip.svg/16px-P_vip.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/69/P_vip.svg/24px-P_vip.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/69/P_vip.svg/31px-P_vip.svg.png 2x" width="16"/>Â <a href="/wiki/Portal:Biography" title="Portal:Biography">Biography portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar" style="background:white; padding:0 5px"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Muhammad" title="Template:Muhammad"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Muhammad" title="Template talk:Muhammad"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Muhammad&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">Muhammad in Islam</a></div>
<p>Following the attestation to the <a href="/wiki/Tawhid" title="Tawhid">oneness of God</a>, the belief in Muhammad's prophethood is the main aspect of the <a href="/wiki/Aqidah" title="Aqidah">Islamic faith</a>. Every Muslim proclaims in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shahadah" title="Shahadah">Shahadah</a></i>: "I testify that there is no god but God, and I testify that Muhammad is a Messenger of God." The Shahadah is the basic creed or tenet of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>. Islamic belief is that ideally the Shahadah is the first words a newborn will hear; children are taught it immediately and it will be recited upon death. Muslims repeat the shahadah in the call to prayer (<i><a href="/wiki/Adhan" title="Adhan">adhan</a></i>) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salat" title="Salat">prayer</a> itself. Non-Muslims wishing to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Islam" title="Conversion to Islam">convert to Islam</a> are required to recite the creed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[263]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Islamic belief, Muhammad is regarded as the last prophet sent by God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-espos12_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-espos12-269">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[265]</a></sup>  Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/10?startingVerse=37" rel="nofollow">10:37</a> states that "...it (the Quran) is a confirmation of (revelations) that went before it, and a fuller explanation of the Bookâwherein there is no doubtâfrom <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">The Lord of the Worlds</a>." Similarly,  Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/46?startingVerse=12" rel="nofollow">46:12</a> states "...And before this was the book of Moses, as a guide and a mercy. And this Book confirms (it)...", while  Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/2?startingVerse=136" rel="nofollow">2:136</a> commands the believers of Islam to "Say: we believe in God and that which is revealed unto us, and that which was revealed unto <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ishmael" title="Ishmael">Ishmael</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isaac" title="Isaac">Isaac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacob" title="Jacob">Jacob</a> and the tribes, and that which <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> received, and which the prophets received from their Lord. We make no distinction between any of them, and unto Him we have surrendered."
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sahadah-Topkapi-Palace.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="395" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Sahadah-Topkapi-Palace.jpg/220px-Sahadah-Topkapi-Palace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Sahadah-Topkapi-Palace.jpg/330px-Sahadah-Topkapi-Palace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Sahadah-Topkapi-Palace.jpg/440px-Sahadah-Topkapi-Palace.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sahadah-Topkapi-Palace.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Profession_(religious)" title="Profession (religious)">profession</a> of faith, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shahadah" title="Shahadah">Shahadah</a>, illustrates the Muslim conception of the role of Muhammad: "There is no god except <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">the God</a>; Muhammad is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" title="Prophets in Islam">Messenger of God</a>." in <a href="/wiki/Topkap%C4%B1_Palace" title="TopkapÄ± Palace">TopkapÄ± Palace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a></div></div></div>
<p>Muslim tradition credits <a href="/wiki/Miracles_of_Muhammad" title="Miracles of Muhammad">Muhammad with several miracles or supernatural events</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Miracle_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Miracle-271">[266]</a></sup> For example, many Muslim commentators and some Western scholars have interpreted the Surah  Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/54?startingVerse=1" rel="nofollow">54:1â2</a> as referring to Muhammad <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Splitting_of_the_moon" title="Splitting of the moon">splitting the Moon</a> in view of the Quraysh when they began persecuting his followers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoQ-Miracle_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-EoQ-Miracle-272">[267]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[268]</a></sup> Western historian of Islam Denis Gril believes the Quran does not overtly describe Muhammad performing <a href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_miracles" title="Islamic view of miracles">miracles</a>, and the supreme miracle of Muhammad is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quran_and_miracles" title="Quran and miracles">identified with the Quran itself</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoQ-Miracle_272-1"><a href="#cite_note-EoQ-Miracle-272">[267]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Islamic tradition, Muhammad was attacked by the people of Ta'if and was badly injured. The tradition also describes an angel appearing to him and offering retribution against the assailants. It is said that Muhammad rejected the offer and prayed for the guidance of the people of Ta'if.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[269]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mohamed_peace_be_upon_him.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="19" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mohamed_peace_be_upon_him.svg/200px-Mohamed_peace_be_upon_him.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mohamed_peace_be_upon_him.svg/300px-Mohamed_peace_be_upon_him.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mohamed_peace_be_upon_him.svg/400px-Mohamed_peace_be_upon_him.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mohamed_peace_be_upon_him.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Calligraphic rendering of "may God honor him and grant him peace", customarily added after Muhammad's name, encoded as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Typographic_ligature" title="Typographic ligature">ligature</a> at <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> code point <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script_in_Unicode" title="Arabic script in Unicode">U+FDFA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unicode_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-unicode-275">[270]</a></sup> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097707674">.mw-parser-output .script-arabic{font-family:"SF Arabic",Scheherazade,Lateef,LateefGR,Amiri,"Noto Naskh Arabic","Droid Arabic Naskh","Scheherazade New","Microsoft Uighur","Sakkal Majalla",Harmattan,"Arabic Typesetting","Arabic Transparent","Times New Roman",Arial,Calibri,"Microsoft Sans Serif","Segoe UI",serif,sans-serif;font-weight:normal}</style><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">ï·º</span>â.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">Sunnah</a> represents actions and sayings of Muhammad (preserved in reports known as <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a>) and covers a broad array of activities and beliefs ranging from religious rituals, personal hygiene, and burial of the dead to the mystical questions involving the love between humans and God. The Sunnah is considered a model of emulation for pious Muslims and has to a great degree influenced the Muslim culture. The greeting that Muhammad taught Muslims to offer each other, "may peace be upon you" (Arabic: <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/As-Salamu_Alaykum" title="As-Salamu Alaykum">as-salamu 'alaykum</a></i>) is used by Muslims throughout the world. Many details of major Islamic rituals such as daily prayers, the fasting and the annual pilgrimage are only found in the Sunnah and not the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[271]</a></sup>
</p><p>Muslims have traditionally expressed love and veneration for Muhammad. Stories of Muhammad's life, his intercession and of his miracles have permeated popular Muslim thought and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Na%60at" title="Na`at">poetry</a>. Among Arabic odes to Muhammad, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qa%E1%B9%A3%C4%ABda_al-Burda" title="Qaá¹£Ä«da al-Burda">Qasidat al-Burda</a> ("Poem of the Mantle") by the Egyptian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> <a href="/wiki/Al-Busiri" title="Al-Busiri">al-Busiri</a> (1211â1294) is particularly well-known, and widely held to possess a healing, spiritual power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stetkevych2010_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stetkevych2010-277">[272]</a></sup> The Quran refers to Muhammad as "a mercy (<i>rahmat</i>) to the worlds" (Quran  Quran <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/21?startingVerse=107" rel="nofollow">21:107</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-27"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup> The association of rain with mercy in Oriental countries has led to imagining Muhammad as a rain cloud dispensing blessings and stretching over lands, reviving the dead hearts, just as rain revives the seemingly dead earth (see, for example, the Sindhi poem of Shah Ê¿Abd al-Latif).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-28"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mawlid" title="Mawlid">Muhammad's birthday</a> is celebrated as a major feast throughout the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Islamic world</a>, excluding <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabi</a>-dominated Saudi Arabia where these public celebrations are discouraged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nasr-Muhammad_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nasr-Muhammad-278">[273]</a></sup> When Muslims say or write the name of Muhammad, they usually follow it with the Arabic phrase <i>á¹£allÄ llahu Ê¿alayhi wa-sallam</i> (<i>may God honor him and grant him peace</i>) or the English phrase <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peace_be_upon_him_(Islam)" title="Peace be upon him (Islam)">peace be upon him</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ann_Goldman_2006_p._212_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ann_Goldman_2006_p._212-279">[274]</a></sup> In casual writing, the abbreviations SAW (for the Arabic phrase) or PBUH (for the English phrase) are sometimes used; in printed matter, a small calligraphic rendition is commonly used (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">ï·º</span></span>).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sufism">Sufism</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a></div>
<p>The Sunnah contributed much to the development of Islamic law, particularly from the end of the first Islamic century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[275]</a></sup>
Muslim mystics, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">sufis</a>, who were seeking for the inner meaning of the Quran and the inner nature of Muhammad, viewed the prophet of Islam not only as a prophet but also as a perfect human being. All Sufi orders trace their chain of spiritual descent back to Muhammad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[276]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Depictions"><span class="anchor" id="Islamic_depictions_of_Muhammad"></span> Depictions</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Depictions_of_Muhammad" title="Depictions of Muhammad">Depictions of Muhammad</a></div>
<p>In line with the <a href="/wiki/Aniconism_in_Islam" title="Aniconism in Islam">hadith's prohibition against creating images of sentient living beings</a>, which is particularly strictly observed with respect to God and Muhammad, Islamic religious art is focused on the word.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plas1987_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plas1987-282">[277]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esposito2011_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-Esposito2011-283">[278]</a></sup> Muslims generally avoid <a href="/wiki/Depictions_of_Muhammad" title="Depictions of Muhammad">depictions of Muhammad</a>, and mosques are decorated with calligraphy and Quranic inscriptions or geometrical designs, not images or sculptures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plas1987_282-1"><a href="#cite_note-Plas1987-282">[277]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peters2010_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peters2010-284">[279]</a></sup> Today, the interdiction against images of Muhammadâdesigned to prevent worship of Muhammad, rather than Godâis much more strictly observed in Sunni Islam (85%â90% of Muslims) and <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya" title="Ahmadiyya">Ahmadiyya</a> Islam (1%) than among Shias (10%â15%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Safi20102010_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-Safi20102010-285">[280]</a></sup> While both Sunnis and Shias have created images of Muhammad in the past,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Omid_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-Omid-286">[281]</a></sup> Islamic depictions of Muhammad are rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plas1987_282-2"><a href="#cite_note-Plas1987-282">[277]</a></sup> They have mostly been limited to the private and elite medium of the miniature, and since about 1500 most depictions show Muhammad with his face veiled, or symbolically represent him as a flame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peters2010_284-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peters2010-284">[279]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bakker2009_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bakker2009-287">[282]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_destroying_idols_-_L%27Histoire_Merveilleuse_en_Vers_de_Mahomet_BNF.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2101" data-file-width="2578" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Muhammad_destroying_idols_-_L%27Histoire_Merveilleuse_en_Vers_de_Mahomet_BNF.jpg/220px-Muhammad_destroying_idols_-_L%27Histoire_Merveilleuse_en_Vers_de_Mahomet_BNF.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Muhammad_destroying_idols_-_L%27Histoire_Merveilleuse_en_Vers_de_Mahomet_BNF.jpg/330px-Muhammad_destroying_idols_-_L%27Histoire_Merveilleuse_en_Vers_de_Mahomet_BNF.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Muhammad_destroying_idols_-_L%27Histoire_Merveilleuse_en_Vers_de_Mahomet_BNF.jpg/440px-Muhammad_destroying_idols_-_L%27Histoire_Merveilleuse_en_Vers_de_Mahomet_BNF.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_destroying_idols_-_L%27Histoire_Merveilleuse_en_Vers_de_Mahomet_BNF.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Muhammad's entry into Mecca and the destruction of idols. Muhammad is shown as a flame in this manuscript. Found in Bazil's <i>Hamla-i Haydari</i>, <a href="/wiki/Jammu_and_Kashmir_(princely_state)" title="Jammu and Kashmir (princely state)">Jammu and Kashmir</a>, India, 1808.</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest extant depictions come from 13th century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatolian_Seljuks" title="Anatolian Seljuks">Anatolian Seljuk</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ilkhanid" title="Ilkhanid">Ilkhanid</a> <a href="/wiki/Persian_miniature" title="Persian miniature">Persian miniatures</a>, typically in literary genres describing the life and deeds of Muhammad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bakker2009_287-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bakker2009-287">[282]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gruber2009_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gruber2009-288">[283]</a></sup> During the Ilkhanid period, when Persia's Mongol rulers converted to Islam, competing Sunni and Shi'a groups used visual imagery, including images of Muhammad, to promote their particular interpretation of Islam's key events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elverskog2010_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elverskog2010-289">[284]</a></sup> Influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a> tradition of representational religious art predating the Mongol elite's conversion, this innovation was unprecedented in the Islamic world, and accompanied by a "broader shift in Islamic artistic culture away from abstraction toward representation" in "mosques, on tapestries, silks, ceramics, and in glass and metalwork" besides books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[285]</a></sup> In the Persian lands, this tradition of realistic depictions lasted through the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_dynasty" title="Timurid dynasty">Timurid dynasty</a> until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safavids" title="Safavids">Safavids</a> took power in the early 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elverskog2010_289-1"><a href="#cite_note-Elverskog2010-289">[284]</a></sup> The Safavaids, who made Shi'i Islam the state religion, initiated a departure from the traditional Ilkhanid and Timurid artistic style by covering Muhammad's face with a veil to obscure his features and at the same time represent his luminous essence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gruber2011_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gruber2011-291">[286]</a></sup> Concomitantly, some of the unveiled images from earlier periods were defaced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elverskog2010_289-2"><a href="#cite_note-Elverskog2010-289">[284]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EdwardsBhaumik2008_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-EdwardsBhaumik2008-292">[287]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruggles2011_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruggles2011-293">[288]</a></sup> Later images were produced in <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> Turkey and elsewhere, but mosques were never decorated with images of Muhammad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Omid_286-1"><a href="#cite_note-Omid-286">[281]</a></sup> Illustrated accounts of the night journey (<i>mi'raj</i>) were particularly popular from the Ilkhanid period through the Safavid era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GruberColby2010_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-GruberColby2010-294">[289]</a></sup> During the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> saw a boom of printed and illustrated <i>mi'raj</i> books, with Muhammad's face veiled, aimed in particular at illiterates and children in the manner of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graphic_novels" title="Graphic novels">graphic novels</a>. Reproduced through <a href="/wiki/Lithography" title="Lithography">lithography</a>, these were essentially "printed manuscripts".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GruberColby2010_294-1"><a href="#cite_note-GruberColby2010-294">[289]</a></sup> Today, millions of historical reproductions and modern images are available in some Muslim-majority countries, especially Turkey and Iran, on posters, postcards, and even in coffee-table books, but are unknown in most other parts of the Islamic world, and when encountered by Muslims from other countries, they can cause considerable consternation and offense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Omid_286-2"><a href="#cite_note-Omid-286">[281]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bakker2009_287-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bakker2009-287">[282]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_appreciation">European appreciation</span></h3>
<div class="depiction">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La.Vie.de.Mahomet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="559" data-file-width="362" decoding="async" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/La.Vie.de.Mahomet.jpg/170px-La.Vie.de.Mahomet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/La.Vie.de.Mahomet.jpg/255px-La.Vie.de.Mahomet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/La.Vie.de.Mahomet.jpg/340px-La.Vie.de.Mahomet.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La.Vie.de.Mahomet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Muhammad in <i>La vie de Mahomet</i> by M. Prideaux (1699). He holds a sword and a crescent while trampling on a <a href="/wiki/Globe" title="Globe">globe</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Christian_cross" title="Christian cross">cross</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Ten Commandments</a>.</div></div></div>
</div>
<p>After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">Reformation</a>, Muhammad was often portrayed in a similar way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-29"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_2002_45_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis_2002_45-295">[290]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Postel" title="Guillaume Postel">Guillaume Postel</a> was among the first to present a more positive view of Muhammad when he argued that Muhammad should be esteemed by Christians as a valid prophet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-30"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[291]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Leibniz">Gottfried Leibniz</a> praised Muhammad because "he did not deviate from the natural religion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-31"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henri_de_Boulainvilliers" title="Henri de Boulainvilliers">Henri de Boulainvilliers</a>, in his <i>Vie de Mahomed</i> which was published posthumously in 1730, described Muhammad as a gifted political leader and a just lawmaker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-32"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup> He presents him as a divinely inspired messenger whom God employed to confound the bickering Oriental Christians, to liberate the Orient from the despotic rule of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Romans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Persians</a>, and to spread the knowledge of the unity of God from India to Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tolan_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-tolan-297">[292]</a></sup> Voltaire had a somewhat mixed opinion on Muhammad: in his play <i><a href="/wiki/Mahomet_(play)" title="Mahomet (play)">Le fanatisme, ou Mahomet le ProphÃ¨te</a></i> he vilifies Muhammad as a symbol of fanaticism, and in a published essay in 1748 he calls him "a sublime and hearty charlatan", but in his historical survey <i>Essai sur les mÅurs</i>, he presents him as legislator and a conqueror and calls him an "enthusiast."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tolan_297-1"><a href="#cite_note-tolan-297">[292]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>, in his <i><a href="/wiki/The_Social_Contract" title="The Social Contract">Social Contract</a></i> (1762), "brushing aside hostile legends of Muhammad as a trickster and impostor, presents him as a sage legislator who wisely fused religious and political powers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tolan_297-2"><a href="#cite_note-tolan-297">[292]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Claude-Emmanuel_de_Pastoret" title="Claude-Emmanuel de Pastoret">Emmanuel Pastoret</a> published in 1787 his <i>Zoroaster, Confucius and Muhammad</i>, in which he presents the lives of these three "great men", "the greatest legislators of the universe", and compares their careers as religious reformers and lawgivers. He rejects the common view that Muhammad is an impostor and argues that the Quran proffers "the most sublime truths of cult and morals"; it defines the unity of God with an "admirable concision." Pastoret writes that the common accusations of his immorality are unfounded: on the contrary, his law enjoins sobriety, generosity, and compassion on his followers: the "legislator of Arabia" was "a great man."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tolan_297-3"><a href="#cite_note-tolan-297">[292]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> admired Muhammad and Islam,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[293]</a></sup> and described him as a model lawmaker and a great man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brockopp_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brockopp-299">[294]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[295]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Carlyle" title="Thomas Carlyle">Thomas Carlyle</a> in his book <i><a href="/wiki/On_Heroes,_Hero-Worship,_%26_the_Heroic_in_History" title="On Heroes, Hero-Worship, &amp; the Heroic in History">On Heroes, Hero-Worship, &amp; the Heroic in History</a></i> (1841) describes "Mahomet" as "A silent great soul; he was one of those who cannot <i>but</i> be in earnest".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[296]</a></sup> Carlyle's interpretation has been widely cited by Muslim scholars as a demonstration that Western scholarship validates Muhammad's status as a great man in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[297]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ian_Almond" title="Ian Almond">Ian Almond</a> says that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Romantic" title="German Romantic">German Romantic</a> writers generally held positive views of Muhammad: "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goethe" title="Goethe">Goethe</a>âs 'extraordinary' poet-prophet, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_Herder" title="Johann Gottfried Herder">Herder</a>âs nation builder (...) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karl_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Schlegel" title="Karl Wilhelm Friedrich Schlegel">Schlegel</a>âs admiration for Islam as an aesthetic product, enviably authentic, radiantly holistic, played such a central role in his view of Mohammed as an exemplary world-fashioner that he even used it as a scale of judgement for the classical (the dithyramb, we are told, has to radiate pure beauty if it is to resemble 'a Koran of poetry')."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[298]</a></sup> After quoting <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Heine" title="Heinrich Heine">Heinrich Heine</a>, who said in a letter to some friend that "I must admit that you, great prophet of Mecca, are the greatest poet and that your Quran... will not easily escape my memory", <a href="/wiki/John_V._Tolan" title="John V. Tolan">John Tolan</a> goes on to show how <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> in Europe in particular held more nuanced views about Muhammad and Islam, being an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnoreligious" title="Ethnoreligious">ethnoreligious</a> minority feeling discriminated, they specifically lauded <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a>, and thus, "writing about Islam was for Jews a way of indulging in a fantasy world, far from the persecution and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pogroms" title="Pogroms">pogroms</a> of nineteenth-century Europe, where Jews could live in harmony with their non-Jewish neighbors."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[299]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent writers such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Montgomery_Watt" title="William Montgomery Watt">William Montgomery Watt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bell_(Arabist)" title="Richard Bell (Arabist)">Richard Bell</a> dismiss the idea that Muhammad deliberately deceived his followers, arguing that Muhammad "was absolutely sincere and acted in complete good faith"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[300]</a></sup> and Muhammad's readiness to endure hardship for his cause, with what seemed to be no rational basis for hope, shows his sincerity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[301]</a></sup> Watt, however, says that sincerity does not directly imply correctness: in contemporary terms, Muhammad might have mistaken his subconscious for divine revelation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[302]</a></sup> Watt and <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Lewis" title="Bernard Lewis">Bernard Lewis</a> argue that viewing Muhammad as a self-seeking impostor makes it impossible to understand Islam's development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[303]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[304]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alford_T._Welch" title="Alford T. Welch">Alford T. Welch</a> holds that Muhammad was able to be so influential and successful because of his firm belief in his vocation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoI-Muhammad_18-33"><a href="#cite_note-EoI-Muhammad-18">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_religions">Other religions</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Jewish_views_on_Muhammad" title="Jewish views on Muhammad">Jewish views on Muhammad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_the_Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="Muhammad in the BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­ Faith">Muhammad in the BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­ Faith</a></div>
<p>Followers of the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­ Faith">BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­ Faith</a> venerate Muhammad as one of a number of prophets or "<a href="/wiki/Manifestation_of_God_(Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith)" title="Manifestation of God (BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­ Faith)">Manifestations of God</a>". He is thought to be the final manifestation, or seal of the <a href="/wiki/Progressive_revelation_(Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD)" title="Progressive revelation (BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­)">Adamic cycle</a>, but consider his teachings to have been superseded by those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27u%27ll%C3%A1h" title="BahÃ¡'u'llÃ¡h">BahÃ¡'u'llÃ¡h</a>, the founder of the BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­ faith, and the first manifestation of the current cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[305]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[306]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a> tradition honors several "mentors" and "prophets",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[307]</a></sup> and Muhammad is considered an important prophet of God in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Druze_faith" title="Druze faith">Druze faith</a>, being among the seven prophets who appeared in different periods of history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hitti_1928_37_313-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hitti_1928_37-313">[308]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dana_2008_17_314-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dana_2008_17-314">[309]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Muhammad" title="Criticism of Muhammad">Criticism of Muhammad</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Muhammad" title="Criticism of Muhammad">Criticism of Muhammad</a> has existed since the 7th century, when Muhammad was decried by his <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_pre-Islamic_Arabia" title="Religion in pre-Islamic Arabia">non-Muslim Arab</a> contemporaries for preaching <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheism</a>, and by the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_tribes_of_Arabia" title="Jewish tribes of Arabia">Jewish tribes of Arabia</a> for his perceived appropriation of <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Biblical narratives</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblical_Figures" title="Biblical Figures">figures</a> and proclamation of himself as the "<a href="/wiki/Seal_of_the_Prophets" title="Seal of the Prophets">Seal of the Prophets</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JE2_315-0"><a href="#cite_note-JE2-315">[310]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stillman_316-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stillman-316">[311]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[312]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, various <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Christianity in the Middle Ages">Western</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">Christian thinkers</a> criticized Muhammad's morality, and labelled him a <a href="/wiki/False_prophet#Christianity" title="False prophet">false prophet</a> or even the <a href="/wiki/Antichrist" title="Antichrist">Antichrist</a>, and he was frequently portrayed in <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a> as being either a <a href="/wiki/Heresy_in_Christianity" title="Heresy in Christianity">heretic</a> or as being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demonic_possession" title="Demonic possession">possessed</a> by <a href="/wiki/Demon#Christianity" title="Demon">demons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quinn2008_318-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quinn2008-318">[313]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goddard2000_319-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goddard2000-319">[314]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curtis2009_320-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curtis2009-320">[315]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buhl1993_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buhl1993-321">[316]</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern religious and secular <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Islam" title="Criticism of Islam">criticism of Islam</a> has concerned Muhammad's sincerity in claiming to be a prophet, his morality, his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad%27s_wives" title="Muhammad's wives">marriages</a>, his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Islam" title="Slavery in Islam">ownership of slaves</a>, his treatment of his enemies, his handling of doctrinal matters and his psychological condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quinn2008_318-1"><a href="#cite_note-Quinn2008-318">[313]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cimino_322-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cimino-322">[317]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willis2013_323-0"><a href="#cite_note-Willis2013-323">[318]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[319]</a></sup>
</p>

An alternative view holds that his status as such does not preclude forms of subordinate prophecy upholding Quranic law to subsist within Islam, a view espoused most notably by the modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya_movement" title="Ahmadiyya movement">Ahmadiyya movement</a>. <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Citations">Citations</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width" style="column-width: 30em;">
<ol class="references">

<li id="cite_note-254"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-254">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFBarlas2012">Barlas, Asma (2012). <i>"Believing Women" in Islam: Unreading Patriarchal Interpretations of the Qur'an</i>. University of Texas Press. p.Â 126. <q>On the other hand, however, Muslims who calculate 'Ayesha's age based on details of her sister Asma's age, about whom more is known, as well as on details of the Hijra (the Prophet's migration from Mecca to Madina), maintain that she was over thirteen and perhaps between seventeen and nineteen when she got married. Such views cohere with those Ahadith that claim that at her marriage Ayesha had "good knowledge of Ancient Arabic poetry and genealogy" and "pronounced the fundamental rules of Arabic Islamic ethics.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=%22Believing+Women%22+in+Islam%3A+Unreading+Patriarchal+Interpretations+of+the+Qur%27an&amp;rft.pages=126&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Texas+Press&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.aulast=Barlas&amp;rft.aufirst=Asma&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMuhammad"></span></span>
</li>
